	<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">New Testament</h1>
	
	<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">
		<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
		<div id="contentSub"></div>
		
		
		
		<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
		<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
		<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>
		<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Second division of the Christian biblical canon</div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">This article is about the Christian Greek Scriptures of the biblical canon. For the theological concept, see <a href="/wiki/New_Covenant" title="New Covenant">New Covenant</a>.</div>

</tr><tr><td style="padding:0.3em 0.4em 0.3em;font-weight:bold;border-top: 1px solid #aaa; border-bottom: 1px solid #aaa;">
<a href="/wiki/File:P_christianity.svg" class="image" title="Christian cross"><img alt="Christian cross" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/P_christianity.svg/20px-P_christianity.svg.png" decoding="async" width="20" height="18" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/P_christianity.svg/30px-P_christianity.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/P_christianity.svg/40px-P_christianity.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="400" data-file-height="360" /></a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Portal:Christianity" title="Portal:Christianity">Christianity portal</a></td></tr><tr><td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%;padding-top: 0.6em;"><div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Christianity_sidebar" title="Template:Christianity sidebar"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Christianity_sidebar" title="Template talk:Christianity sidebar"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Christianity_sidebar&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>The <b>New Testament</b> (<a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Ancient Greek language">Ancient Greek</a>: <span lang="grc">Ἡ Καινὴ Διαθήκη</span>, <a href="/wiki/Transliteration" title="Transliteration">transl.</a> <i lang="grc-Latn" title="Ancient Greek language text">Hē Kainḕ Diathḗkē</i>; <a href="/wiki/Latin_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Latin language">Latin</a>: <i lang="la">Novum Testamentum</i>) is the second part of the <a href="/wiki/Christian_biblical_canons" title="Christian biblical canons">Christian biblical canon</a>, the first being the <a href="/wiki/Old_Testament" title="Old Testament">Old Testament</a>. The New Testament discusses the teachings and person of <a href="/wiki/Jesus_in_Christianity" title="Jesus in Christianity">Jesus</a>, as well as events in <a href="/wiki/Christianity_in_the_1st_century" title="Christianity in the 1st century">first-century Christianity</a>. Christians regard both the Old and New Testaments together as <a href="/wiki/Religious_text" title="Religious text">sacred scripture</a>.
</p><p>The New Testament is a collection of Christian texts originally written in the <a href="/wiki/Koine_Greek" title="Koine Greek">Koine Greek</a> language, at different times by various different authors. While the Old Testament canon varies somewhat between different <a href="/wiki/Christian_denomination" title="Christian denomination">Christian denominations</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Development_of_the_New_Testament_canon" title="Development of the New Testament canon">27-book canon</a> of the New Testament has been almost universally recognized within <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christianity</a> since at least <a href="/wiki/Late_Antiquity" class="mw-redirect" title="Late Antiquity">Late Antiquity</a>. Thus, in almost all Christian traditions today, the New Testament consists of 27 books: the four <a href="/wiki/Canonical_gospels" class="mw-redirect" title="Canonical gospels">canonical gospels</a> (<a href="/wiki/Gospel_of_Matthew" title="Gospel of Matthew">Matthew</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gospel_of_Mark" title="Gospel of Mark">Mark</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gospel_of_Luke" title="Gospel of Luke">Luke</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Gospel_of_John" title="Gospel of John">John</a>), the <a href="/wiki/Acts_of_the_Apostles" title="Acts of the Apostles">Acts of the Apostles</a>, the fourteen <a href="/wiki/Pauline_epistles" title="Pauline epistles">epistles of Paul</a>, the seven <a href="/wiki/Catholic_epistles" title="Catholic epistles">catholic epistles</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Revelation" title="Book of Revelation">Book of Revelation</a>.
</p><p>The earliest known complete list of the 27 books of the New Testament is found in a letter written by <a href="/wiki/Athanasius" class="mw-redirect" title="Athanasius">Athanasius</a>, a 4th-century <a href="/wiki/Bishop" title="Bishop">bishop</a> of <a href="/wiki/Alexandria" title="Alexandria">Alexandria</a>, dated to 367 AD.<sup id="cite_ref-LindbergCarter_1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LindbergCarter-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> The 27-book New Testament was first formally canonized during the councils of <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Hippo" class="mw-redirect" title="Council of Hippo">Hippo</a> (393) and <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Carthage" class="mw-redirect" title="Council of Carthage">Carthage</a> (397) in North Africa. <a href="/wiki/Pope" title="Pope">Pope</a> <a href="/wiki/Innocent_I" class="mw-redirect" title="Innocent I">Innocent I</a> ratified the same canon in 405, but it is probable that a Council in Rome in 382 under <a href="/wiki/Pope_Damasus_I" title="Pope Damasus I">Pope Damasus I</a> gave the same list first. These councils also provided the canon of the Old Testament, which included the <a href="/wiki/Biblical_apocrypha" title="Biblical apocrypha">apocryphal</a> books.<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>There is no <a href="/wiki/Scholarly_consensus" class="mw-redirect" title="Scholarly consensus">scholarly consensus</a> on the date of composition of the latest New Testament texts. Conservative scholars <a href="/wiki/John_Robinson_(bishop_of_Woolwich)#Redating_the_New_Testament_(1976)" title="John Robinson (bishop of Woolwich)">John A. T. Robinson</a>, <a href="/wiki/Daniel_B._Wallace" title="Daniel B. Wallace">Dan Wallace</a>, and <a href="/wiki/William_F._Albright" title="William F. Albright">William F. Albright</a> dated all the books of the New Testament before 70 AD.<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> But most scholars date some New Testament texts much later than this. For example, <a href="/wiki/Richard_Pervo" title="Richard Pervo">Richard Pervo</a> dates <a href="/wiki/Luke-Acts" class="mw-redirect" title="Luke-Acts">Luke-Acts</a> to c. AD 115,<sup id="cite_ref-pervo_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pervo-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/David_Trobisch" title="David Trobisch">David Trobisch</a> places <a href="/wiki/Acts_of_the_Apostles" title="Acts of the Apostles">Acts</a> in the mid- to late second century, contemporaneous with the publication of the first New Testament canon.<sup id="cite_ref-trobisch-2007_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-trobisch-2007-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;note 1&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Testament&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Etymology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The use of the phrase <i>New Testament</i> (<a href="/wiki/Koine_Greek" title="Koine Greek">Koine Greek</a>: <span lang="grc" title="Ancient Greek language text">Ἡ Καινὴ Διαθήκη</span>, <i lang="grc-Latn" title="Ancient Greek language text">Hē Kainḕ Diathḗkē</i>) to describe a collection of first and second-century Christian Greek scriptures can be traced back to <a href="/wiki/Tertullian" title="Tertullian">Tertullian</a> in his work <i>Against <a href="/wiki/Praxeas" title="Praxeas">Praxeas</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-trobisch-2000_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-trobisch-2000-7">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-trobisch-2012_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-trobisch-2012-8">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-praxeas_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-praxeas-9">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> Irenaeus uses the phrase "New Testament" several times, but does not use it in reference to any written text.<sup id="cite_ref-trobisch-2012_8-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-trobisch-2012-8">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> In <i>Against <a href="/wiki/Marcion" class="mw-redirect" title="Marcion">Marcion</a></i>, written c.&#160;208&#160;AD, Tertullian writes of:<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r886047036">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>the Divine Word, who is doubly edged with the two testaments of the <a href="/wiki/Torah" title="Torah">law</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Gospel" title="Gospel">gospel</a>.
</p></blockquote>
<p>And Tertullian continues later in the book, writing:<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;note 2&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886047036"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>it is certain that the whole aim at which he [Marcion] has strenuously laboured, even in the drawing up of his <a href="/wiki/Antitheses" class="mw-redirect" title="Antitheses">Antitheses</a>, centres in this, that he may establish a diversity between the Old and the New Testaments, so that his own <a href="/wiki/Christ" class="mw-redirect" title="Christ">Christ</a> may be separate from the <a href="/wiki/Creator_God" class="mw-redirect" title="Creator God">Creator</a>, as belonging to this rival god, and as alien from the law and the <a href="/wiki/Neviim" class="mw-redirect" title="Neviim">prophets</a>.
</p></blockquote>
<p>By the <a href="/wiki/Christianity_in_the_4th_century" title="Christianity in the 4th century">4th century</a>, the existence—even if not the exact contents—of both an Old and New Testament had been established. <a href="/wiki/Lactantius" title="Lactantius">Lactantius</a>, a 3rd–4th century Christian author wrote in his early-4th-century Latin <i>Institutiones Divinae</i> (<i>Divine Institutes</i>):<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886047036"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>But all scripture is divided into two Testaments. That which preceded the advent and passion of Christ—that is, the <a href="/wiki/Torah" title="Torah">law</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Neviim" class="mw-redirect" title="Neviim">prophets</a>—is called the Old; but those things which were written after His resurrection are named the New Testament. The Jews make use of the Old, we of the New: but yet they are not discordant, for the New is the fulfilling of the Old, and in both there is the same testator, even Christ, who, having suffered death for us, made us heirs of His everlasting kingdom, the people of the Jews being deprived and disinherited. As the prophet Jeremiah testifies when he speaks such things: "Behold, the days come, saith the Lord, that I will make a new testament to the house of Israel and the house of Judah, not according to the testament which I made to their fathers, in the day that I took them by the hand to bring them out of the land of Egypt; for they continued not in my testament, and I disregarded them, saith the Lord."<sup class="nourlexpansion reference"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=Jer+31%3A31%E2%80%9332&amp;version=NRSV">&#91;Jer 31:31–32&#93;</a></sup>  ... For that which He said above, that He would make a new testament to the house of Judah, shows that the old testament which was given by Moses was not perfect; but that which was to be given by Christ would be complete.
</p></blockquote>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Books">Books</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Testament&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Books">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Christian_biblical_canons" title="Christian biblical canons">Christian biblical canons</a>, <a href="/wiki/Development_of_the_New_Testament_canon" title="Development of the New Testament canon">Development of the New Testament canon</a>, <a href="/wiki/New_Testament_apocrypha" title="New Testament apocrypha">New Testament apocrypha</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Template:Books_of_the_New_Testament" title="Template:Books of the New Testament">Template:Books of the New Testament</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Gospels">The Gospels</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Testament&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: The Gospels">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Canonical_gospels" class="mw-redirect" title="Canonical gospels">Canonical gospels</a></div>
<p>Each of the four <a href="/wiki/Gospels" class="mw-redirect" title="Gospels">gospels</a> in the New Testament narrates the life, death, and resurrection of <a href="/wiki/Jesus" title="Jesus">Jesus</a> of <a href="/wiki/Nazareth" title="Nazareth">Nazareth</a>. The word "gospel" derives from the <a href="/wiki/Old_English_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Old English language">Old English</a> <i>gōd-spell</i><sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> (rarely <i>godspel</i>), meaning "good news" or "glad tidings". The gospel was considered the "good news" of the coming <a href="/wiki/Messiah#Christianity" title="Messiah">Kingdom of Messiah</a>, and the redemption through the life and death of Jesus, the central Christian message.<sup id="cite_ref-ODCC_self_Gospel_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ODCC_self_Gospel-15">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> Gospel is a <a href="/wiki/Calque" title="Calque">calque</a> (word-for-word translation) of the <a href="/wiki/Koine_Greek" title="Koine Greek">Greek</a> word <span lang="grc" title="Ancient Greek language text">εὐαγγέλιον</span>, <i>euangelion</i> (<i>eu-</i> "good", <i>-angelion</i> "message").
</p><p>Starting in the late second century, the four narrative accounts of the life and work of Jesus Christ have been referred to as "The Gospel of ..." or "The Gospel according to ..." followed by the name of the supposed author. The first author to explicitly name the canonical gospels is <a href="/wiki/Irenaeus" title="Irenaeus">Irenaeus</a> of Lyon,<sup id="cite_ref-trobisch-2012_8-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-trobisch-2012-8">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> who promoted the four canonical gospels in his book <a href="/wiki/Against_Heresies_(Irenaeus)" title="Against Heresies (Irenaeus)">Against Heresies</a>, written around 180.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> Whatever these admittedly early ascriptions may imply about the sources behind or the perception of these gospels, they are anonymous compositions.
</p>
<ul><li>The <a href="/wiki/Gospel_of_Matthew" title="Gospel of Matthew">Gospel of Matthew</a>, ascribed to the <a href="/wiki/Matthew_the_Evangelist" class="mw-redirect" title="Matthew the Evangelist">Apostle Matthew</a>. This gospel begins with a <a href="/wiki/Genealogy_of_Jesus" title="Genealogy of Jesus">genealogy of Jesus</a> and a story of his birth that includes a visit from <a href="/wiki/Biblical_Magi" title="Biblical Magi">magi</a> and a <a href="/wiki/Flight_into_Egypt" title="Flight into Egypt">flight into Egypt</a>, and it ends with the <a href="/wiki/Great_Commission" title="Great Commission">commissioning of the disciples</a> by the resurrected Jesus.</li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Gospel_of_Mark" title="Gospel of Mark">Gospel of Mark</a>, ascribed to <a href="/wiki/Mark_the_Evangelist" title="Mark the Evangelist">Mark the Evangelist</a>. This gospel begins with the preaching of <a href="/wiki/John_the_Baptist" title="John the Baptist">John the Baptist</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Baptism_of_Jesus" title="Baptism of Jesus">baptism of Jesus</a>. Two different secondary endings were affixed to this gospel in the 2nd century.</li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Gospel_of_Luke" title="Gospel of Luke">Gospel of Luke</a>, ascribed to <a href="/wiki/Luke_the_Evangelist" title="Luke the Evangelist">Luke the Evangelist</a>, who was not one of <a href="/wiki/Twelve_Disciples#The_Twelve_Apostles" class="mw-redirect" title="Twelve Disciples">the Twelve Apostles</a>, but was mentioned as a companion of the <a href="/wiki/Paul_of_Tarsus" class="mw-redirect" title="Paul of Tarsus">Apostle Paul</a> and as a physician.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> This gospel begins with parallel stories of the birth and childhood of <a href="/wiki/John_the_Baptist" title="John the Baptist">John the Baptist</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jesus" title="Jesus">Jesus</a> and ends with appearances of the resurrected Jesus and his ascension into heaven.</li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Gospel_of_John" title="Gospel of John">Gospel of John</a>, ascribed to <a href="/wiki/John_the_Evangelist" title="John the Evangelist">John the Evangelist</a>. This gospel begins with a philosophical prologue and ends with appearances of the resurrected Jesus. It is about Jesus's miracles.</li></ul>
<p>The first three gospels listed above are classified as the <a href="/wiki/Synoptic_Gospels" title="Synoptic Gospels">Synoptic Gospels</a>. They contain similar accounts of the events in Jesus's life and his teaching, due to their literary interdependence. The Gospel of John is structured differently and includes stories of several miracles of Jesus and sayings not found in the other three.
</p><p>These four gospels that were eventually included in the New Testament were only a few among many other early Christian gospels. The existence of such texts is even mentioned at the beginning of the Gospel of Luke.<sup class="nourlexpansion reference"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=Luke+1%3A1%E2%80%934&amp;version=NRSV">&#91;Luke 1:1–4&#93;</a></sup> Other early Christian gospels such as the so-called "<a href="/wiki/Jewish-Christian_Gospels" class="mw-redirect" title="Jewish-Christian Gospels">Jewish-Christian Gospels</a>" or the <a href="/wiki/Gospel_of_Thomas" title="Gospel of Thomas">Gospel of Thomas</a>, also offer both a window into the context of <a href="/wiki/Early_Christianity" title="Early Christianity">early Christianity</a> and may provide some assistance in the reconstruction of the <a href="/wiki/Historical_Jesus" title="Historical Jesus">historical Jesus</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Acts_of_the_Apostles">Acts of the Apostles</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Testament&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Acts of the Apostles">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Acts_of_the_Apostles" title="Acts of the Apostles">Acts of the Apostles</a> is a narrative of the apostles' ministry and activity after <a href="/wiki/Christ%27s_death" class="mw-redirect" title="Christ&#39;s death">Christ's death</a> and resurrection, from which point it resumes and functions as a sequel to the <a href="/wiki/Gospel_of_Luke" title="Gospel of Luke">Gospel of Luke</a>. Examining style, phraseology, and other evidence, modern scholarship generally concludes that Acts and the Gospel of Luke share the same author, referred to as <a href="/wiki/Luke%E2%80%93Acts" title="Luke–Acts">Luke–Acts</a>. Luke-Acts does not name its author.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBurkett2002196_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBurkett2002196-19">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> Church tradition identified him as <a href="/wiki/Luke_the_Evangelist" title="Luke the Evangelist">Luke the Evangelist</a>, the companion of Paul, but the majority of scholars reject this due to the many differences between Acts and the authentic Pauline letters.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEhrman2005235_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEhrman2005235-20">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> The most probable date of composition is around 80–100 AD, although some scholars date it significantly later,<sup id="cite_ref-pervo_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pervo-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-trobisch-2007_5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-trobisch-2007-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> and there is evidence that it was still being substantially revised well into the 2nd century.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPerkins2009250–53_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPerkins2009250–53-21">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Epistles">Epistles</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Testament&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Epistles">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The epistles of the New Testament are considered by Christians to be divinely inspired and holy letters, written by the apostles and disciples of Christ, to either local congregations with specific needs, or to <a href="/wiki/New_Covenant" title="New Covenant">New Covenant</a> Christians in general, scattered about; or "catholic epistles."
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Pauline_letters_to_churches">Pauline letters to churches</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Testament&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Pauline letters to churches">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Pauline_epistles" title="Pauline epistles">Pauline epistles</a></div>
<p>The Pauline letters to churches are the thirteen New Testament books that present <a href="/wiki/Paul_the_Apostle" title="Paul the Apostle">Paul the Apostle</a> as their author.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;note 3&#93;</a></sup> Six of the letters are disputed. Four are thought by most modern scholars to be <a href="/wiki/Pseudepigraphy" class="mw-redirect" title="Pseudepigraphy">pseudepigraphic</a>, i.e., not actually written by Paul even if attributed to him within the letters themselves.  Opinion is more divided on the other two disputed letters (2 Thessalonians and Colossians).<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> These letters were written to Christian communities in specific cities or geographical regions, often to address issues faced by that particular community. Prominent themes include the relationship both to broader "<a href="/wiki/Gentile" title="Gentile">pagan</a>" society, to Judaism, and to other Christians.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Epistle_to_the_Romans" title="Epistle to the Romans">Epistle to the Romans</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/First_Epistle_to_the_Corinthians" title="First Epistle to the Corinthians">First Epistle to the Corinthians</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Second_Epistle_to_the_Corinthians" title="Second Epistle to the Corinthians">Second Epistle to the Corinthians</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Epistle_to_the_Galatians" title="Epistle to the Galatians">Epistle to the Galatians</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Epistle_to_the_Ephesians" title="Epistle to the Ephesians">Epistle to the Ephesians</a><sup>*</sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Epistle_to_the_Philippians" title="Epistle to the Philippians">Epistle to the Philippians</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Epistle_to_the_Colossians" title="Epistle to the Colossians">Epistle to the Colossians</a><sup>*</sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/First_Epistle_to_the_Thessalonians" title="First Epistle to the Thessalonians">First Epistle to the Thessalonians</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Second_Epistle_to_the_Thessalonians" title="Second Epistle to the Thessalonians">Second Epistle to the Thessalonians</a><sup>*</sup></li></ul>
<p>[Disputed letters are marked with an asterisk (*).]
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Pauline_Letters_to_Persons">Pauline Letters to Persons</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Testament&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Pauline Letters to Persons">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The last four Pauline letters in the New Testament are addressed to individual persons. They include the following:
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/First_Epistle_to_Timothy" title="First Epistle to Timothy">First Epistle to Timothy</a><sup>*</sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Second_Epistle_to_Timothy" title="Second Epistle to Timothy">Second Epistle to Timothy</a><sup>*</sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Epistle_to_Titus" title="Epistle to Titus">Epistle to Titus</a><sup>*</sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Epistle_to_Philemon" title="Epistle to Philemon">Epistle to Philemon</a></li></ul>
<p>[Disputed letters are marked with an asterisk (*).]
</p><p>All of the above except for Philemon are known as the <a href="/wiki/Pastoral_epistles" title="Pastoral epistles">Pastoral epistles</a>. They are addressed to individuals charged with pastoral oversight of churches and discuss issues of Christian living, doctrine and leadership. They often address different concerns to those of the preceding epistles. These letters are believed by many to be pseudepigraphic. Some scholars (e.g., Bill Mounce, Ben Witherington) will argue that the letters are genuinely Pauline, or at least written under Paul's supervision.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Hebrews">Hebrews</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Testament&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Hebrews">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Epistle_to_the_Hebrews" title="Epistle to the Hebrews">Epistle to the Hebrews</a> addresses a Jewish audience who had come to believe that Jesus was the <a href="/wiki/Messiah#Judaism" title="Messiah">anointed one</a> (Hebrew: מָשִׁיחַ—transliterated in English as "Moshiach", or "Messiah"; Greek: Χριστός—transliterated in English as "Christos", for "<a href="/wiki/Christ" class="mw-redirect" title="Christ">Christ</a>") who was predicted in the writings of the Hebrew Scriptures. The author discusses the superiority of the new covenant and the ministry of Jesus, to the <a href="/wiki/Mosaic_covenant" title="Mosaic covenant">Mosaic covenant</a> <sup class="nourlexpansion reference"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=Heb.+1%3A1%E2%80%9310%3A18&amp;version=NRSV">&#91;Heb. 1:1–10:18&#93;</a></sup> and urges the readers in the practical implications of this conviction through the end of the epistle.<sup class="nourlexpansion reference"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=Heb.+10%3A19%E2%80%9313%3A25&amp;version=NRSV">&#91;Heb. 10:19–13:25&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The book has been widely accepted by the Christian church as inspired by God and thus authoritative, despite the acknowledgment of uncertainties about who its human author was. Regarding authorship, although the Epistle to the Hebrews does not internally claim to have been written by the <a href="/wiki/Paul_of_Tarsus" class="mw-redirect" title="Paul of Tarsus">Apostle Paul</a>, some similarities in wordings to some of the Pauline Epistles have been noted and inferred. In antiquity, some began to ascribe it to Paul in an attempt to provide the anonymous work an explicit apostolic pedigree.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the 4th century, <a href="/wiki/Jerome" title="Jerome">Jerome</a> and <a href="/wiki/Augustine_of_Hippo" title="Augustine of Hippo">Augustine of Hippo</a> supported <a href="/wiki/Authorship_of_the_Pauline_epistles" title="Authorship of the Pauline epistles">Paul's authorship</a>. The Church largely agreed to include Hebrews as the fourteenth letter of Paul, and affirmed this authorship until the <a href="/wiki/Protestant_Reformation" class="mw-redirect" title="Protestant Reformation">Reformation</a>. The letter to the Hebrews had difficulty in being accepted as part of the Christian canon because of its anonymity.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> As early as the 3rd century, <a href="/wiki/Origen" title="Origen">Origen</a> wrote of the letter, "Men of old have handed it down as Paul's, but who wrote the Epistle God only knows."<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Contemporary scholars often reject Pauline authorship for the epistle to the Hebrews,<sup id="cite_ref-Ehrman_2004_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ehrman_2004-29">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> based on its distinctive style and theology, which are considered to set it apart from Paul's writings.<sup id="cite_ref-Powell_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Powell-30">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Catholic_epistles">Catholic epistles</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Testament&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Catholic epistles">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Catholic_epistles" title="Catholic epistles">Catholic epistles</a> (or "general epistles") consist of both letters and treatises in the form of letters written to the church at large. The term "<a href="/wiki/Catholic" class="mw-redirect" title="Catholic">catholic</a>" (<a href="/wiki/Koine_Greek" title="Koine Greek">Greek</a>: καθολική, <i>katholikē</i>), used to describe these letters in the oldest manuscripts containing them, here simply means "general" or "universal". The authorship of a number of these is disputed.
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Epistle_of_James" title="Epistle of James">Epistle of James</a>, written by an author named "James", often identified with <a href="/wiki/James_the_Just" class="mw-redirect" title="James the Just">James, the brother of Jesus</a>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/First_Epistle_of_Peter" title="First Epistle of Peter">First Epistle of Peter</a>, ascribed to the <a href="/wiki/Saint_Peter" title="Saint Peter">Apostle Peter</a>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Second_Epistle_of_Peter" title="Second Epistle of Peter">Second Epistle of Peter</a>, ascribed to the Apostle Peter, though widely considered not to have been written by him.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/First_Epistle_of_John" title="First Epistle of John">First Epistle of John</a>, ascribed to <a href="/wiki/John_the_Apostle" title="John the Apostle">John the Apostle</a>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Second_Epistle_of_John" title="Second Epistle of John">Second Epistle of John</a>, ascribed to John the Apostle.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Third_Epistle_of_John" title="Third Epistle of John">Third Epistle of John</a>, ascribed to John the Apostle.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Epistle_of_Jude" title="Epistle of Jude">Epistle of Jude</a>, written under the name of <a href="/wiki/Jude,_brother_of_Jesus" title="Jude, brother of Jesus">Jude, the brother of Jesus and James</a>.</li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Book_of_Revelation">Book of Revelation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Testament&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Book of Revelation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Authorship_of_the_Johannine_works" title="Authorship of the Johannine works">Authorship of the Johannine works</a></div>
<p>The final book of the New Testament is the <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Revelation" title="Book of Revelation">Book of Revelation</a>, also known as the Apocalypse of John. In the New Testament canon, it is considered <a href="/wiki/Bible_prophecy" title="Bible prophecy">prophetical</a> or <a href="/wiki/Apocalyptic_literature" title="Apocalyptic literature">apocalyptic literature</a>. Its authorship has been attributed either to John the Apostle (in which case it is often thought that John the Apostle is <a href="/wiki/John_the_Evangelist" title="John the Evangelist">John the Evangelist</a>, i.e. author of the <a href="/wiki/Gospel_of_John" title="Gospel of John">Gospel of John</a>) or to another John designated "<a href="/wiki/John_of_Patmos" title="John of Patmos">John of Patmos</a>" after the island where the text says the revelation was received (1:9). Some ascribe the writership date as circa 81–96 AD, and others at around 68 AD.<sup id="cite_ref-Mounce_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mounce-32">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> The work opens with letters to <a href="/wiki/Seven_churches_of_Asia" title="Seven churches of Asia">seven local congregations of Asia Minor</a> and thereafter takes the form of an <a href="/wiki/Apocalypse" class="mw-redirect" title="Apocalypse">apocalypse</a>, a "revealing" of divine prophecy and mysteries, a literary genre popular in ancient Judaism and Christianity.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<dl><dt>Table notes</dt></dl>
<div class="mw-references-wrap mw-references-columns"><ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-Armenian-35"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Armenian_35-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The growth and development of the Armenian biblical canon is complex; extra-canonical New Testament books appear in historical canon lists and recensions that are either distinct to this tradition, or where they do exist elsewhere, never achieved the same status.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> Some of the books are not listed in this table; these include the Prayer of <a href="/wiki/Euthalius" title="Euthalius">Euthalius</a>, the Repose of <a href="/wiki/St._John_the_Evangelist" class="mw-redirect" title="St. John the Evangelist">St. John the Evangelist</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Doctrine_of_Addai" title="Doctrine of Addai">Doctrine of Addai</a>, a reading from the <a href="/wiki/Gospel_of_James" title="Gospel of James">Gospel of James</a>, <a href="/wiki/Apostolic_Canons" class="mw-redirect" title="Apostolic Canons">the Second Apostolic Canons</a>, the Words of <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Barsabbas" title="Joseph Barsabbas">Justus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pseudo-Dionysius_the_Areopagite" title="Pseudo-Dionysius the Areopagite">Dionysius Aeropagite</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Preaching_of_Peter" class="mw-redirect" title="Preaching of Peter">Preaching of Peter</a>, and a Poem by <a href="/wiki/Ghazar_Parpetsi" title="Ghazar Parpetsi">Ghazar</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> (Various sources<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> also mention undefined Armenian canonical additions to the Gospels of Mark and John, however, these may refer to the general additions—Mark 16:9–20 and John 7:53–8:11—discussed elsewhere in these notes.) A possible exception here to canonical exclusivity is the Second Apostolic Canons, which share a common source—the <a href="/wiki/Apostolic_Constitutions" title="Apostolic Constitutions">Apostolic Constitutions</a>—with certain parts of the Orthodox Tewahedo New Testament broader canon.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> The <i>Acts of Thaddeus</i> was included in the biblical canon of <a href="/wiki/Gregory_of_Tatev" title="Gregory of Tatev">Gregory of Tatev</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENersessian200129_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENersessian200129-34">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> There is some uncertainty about whether Armenian canon lists include the Doctrine of Addai or the related <a href="/wiki/Acts_of_Thaddeus" title="Acts of Thaddeus">Acts of Thaddeus</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> Moreover, the correspondence between King <a href="/wiki/Agbar" class="mw-redirect" title="Agbar">Agbar</a> and Jesus Christ, which is found in various forms—including within both the Doctrine of Addai and the Acts of Thaddeus—sometimes appears separately (see <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.looys.net/BIBCANON.DOC">list</a><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources#What_information_to_include" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="A complete citation is needed (February 2016)">full citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>). The Prayer of Euthalius and the Repose of St. John the Evangelist appear in the appendix of the 1805 Armenian Zohrab Bible;<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> however, some of the aforementioned books, though they are found within canon lists, have nonetheless never been discovered to be part of any Armenian biblical manuscript.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENersessian200129_34-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENersessian200129-34">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-infancy-36"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-infancy_36-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Though widely regarded as non-canonical,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> the Gospel of James obtained early liturgical acceptance among some Eastern churches and remains a major source for many of Christendom's traditions related to <a href="/wiki/Mary,_the_mother_of_Jesus" class="mw-redirect" title="Mary, the mother of Jesus">Mary, the mother of Jesus</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-Tatian-37"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Tatian_37-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Tatian_37-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Tatian_37-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Tatian_37-3"><sup><i><b>d</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">The <a href="/wiki/Diatessaron" title="Diatessaron">Diatessaron</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tatian" title="Tatian">Tatian</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Gospel_harmony" title="Gospel harmony">gospel harmony</a>, became a standard text in some Syriac-speaking churches down to the 5th century, when it gave-way to the four separate gospels found in the Peshitta.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-Addition-38"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Addition_38-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Addition_38-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Addition_38-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Addition_38-3"><sup><i><b>d</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">Parts of these four books are not found in the most reliable ancient sources; in some cases, are thought to be later additions, and have therefore not appeared historically in every biblical tradition.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> They are as follows: <a href="/wiki/Mark_16" title="Mark 16">Mark 16:9–20</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jesus_and_the_woman_taken_in_adultery" title="Jesus and the woman taken in adultery">John 7:53–8:11</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Comma_Johanneum" class="mw-redirect" title="Comma Johanneum">Comma Johanneum</a>, and portions of the <a href="/wiki/Acts_of_the_Apostles#Manuscripts" title="Acts of the Apostles">Western version of Acts</a>. To varying degrees, arguments for the authenticity of these passages—especially for the one from the Gospel of John—have occasionally been made.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-Goth-39"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Goth_39-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="/wiki/Skeireins" title="Skeireins">Skeireins</a>, a commentary on the Gospel of John in the <a href="/wiki/Gothic_language" title="Gothic language">Gothic language</a>, was included in the <a href="/wiki/Wulfila_Bible" class="mw-redirect" title="Wulfila Bible">Wulfila Bible</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> It exists today only in fragments.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-AoP-40"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-AoP_40-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-AoP_40-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">The Acts of Paul and Thecla, the Epistle of the Corinthians to Paul, and the Third Epistle to the Corinthians are all portions of the greater <a href="/wiki/Acts_of_Paul" title="Acts of Paul">Acts of Paul</a> narrative, which is part of a stichometric catalogue of New Testament canon found in the <a href="/wiki/Codex_Claromontanus" title="Codex Claromontanus">Codex Claromontanus</a>, but has survived only in fragments.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> Some of the content within these individual sections may have developed separately.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-Luther-43"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Luther_43-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Luther_43-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Luther_43-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Luther_43-3"><sup><i><b>d</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">These four works were questioned or "<a href="/wiki/Antilegomena" title="Antilegomena">spoken against</a>" by <a href="/wiki/Martin_Luther" title="Martin Luther">Martin Luther</a>, and he changed the order of <a href="/wiki/Luther_Bible" title="Luther Bible">his New Testament</a> to reflect this, but he did not leave them out, nor has any <a href="/wiki/Lutheranism" title="Lutheranism">Lutheran</a> body since.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> Traditional German Luther Bibles are still printed with the New Testament in this changed "Lutheran" order.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> The vast majority of Protestants embrace these four works as fully canonical.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-Syriac-44"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Syriac_44-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Syriac_44-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Syriac_44-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Syriac_44-3"><sup><i><b>d</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Syriac_44-4"><sup><i><b>e</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">The Peshitta excludes 2 John, 3 John, 2 Peter, Jude, and Revelation, but certain Bibles of the modern Syriac traditions include later translations of those books.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> Still today, the official <a href="/wiki/Lectionary" title="Lectionary">lectionary</a> followed by the Syriac Orthodox Church and the Assyrian Church of the East presents lessons from only the twenty-two books of Peshitta, the version to which appeal is made for the settlement of doctrinal questions.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-Corinthians-45"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Corinthians_45-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The Third Epistle to the Corinthians often appears with and is framed as a response to the <a href="/wiki/Epistle_of_the_Corinthians_to_Paul" title="Epistle of the Corinthians to Paul">Epistle of the Corinthians to Paul</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-Laodiceans-46"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Laodiceans_46-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The Epistle to the Laodiceans is present in some western non-Roman Catholic translations and traditions.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Quakers" title="Quakers">Quakers</a>' use of it to the point where they produced a translation and made pleas for its canonicity, see Poole's <i>Annotations</i>, on Col. 4:16. The epistle is nonetheless widely rejected by the vast majority of Protestants.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-ApocPeter-48"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-ApocPeter_48-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The <a href="/wiki/Apocalypse_of_Peter" title="Apocalypse of Peter">Apocalypse of Peter</a>, though not listed in this table, is mentioned in the <a href="/wiki/Muratorian_fragment" title="Muratorian fragment">Muratorian fragment</a> and is part of a stichometric catalogue of New Testament canon found in the Codex Claromontanus.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> It was also held in high regard by <a href="/wiki/Clement_of_Alexandria" title="Clement of Alexandria">Clement of Alexandria</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-Fathers-49"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Fathers_49-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Other known writings of the Apostolic Fathers not listed in this table are as follows: the seven <a href="/wiki/Epistles_of_Ignatius" class="mw-redirect" title="Epistles of Ignatius">Epistles of Ignatius</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Epistle_of_Polycarp" class="mw-redirect" title="Epistle of Polycarp">Epistle of Polycarp</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Martyrdom_of_Polycarp" title="Martyrdom of Polycarp">Martyrdom of Polycarp</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Epistle_to_Diognetus" title="Epistle to Diognetus">Epistle to Diognetus</a>, the fragment of <a href="/wiki/Quadratus_of_Athens" title="Quadratus of Athens">Quadratus of Athens</a>, the fragments of <a href="/wiki/Papias_of_Hierapolis" title="Papias of Hierapolis">Papias of Hierapolis</a>, the Reliques of the Elders Preserved in <a href="/wiki/Irenaeus" title="Irenaeus">Irenaeus</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Apostles%27_Creed" title="Apostles&#39; Creed">Apostles' Creed</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup></span>

<li id="cite_note-Apostloic-50"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Apostloic_50-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Though they are not listed in this table, the <a href="/wiki/Apostolic_Constitutions" title="Apostolic Constitutions">Apostolic Constitutions</a> were considered canonical by some including <a href="/wiki/Alexius_Aristenus" class="mw-redirect" title="Alexius Aristenus">Alexius Aristenus</a>, <a href="/wiki/John_of_Salisbury" title="John of Salisbury">John of Salisbury</a>, and to a lesser extent, <a href="/wiki/Grigor_Tatevatsi" class="mw-redirect" title="Grigor Tatevatsi">Grigor Tat`evatsi</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-ApFa-51"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-ApFa_51-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-ApFa_51-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-ApFa_51-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-ApFa_51-3"><sup><i><b>d</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-ApFa_51-4"><sup><i><b>e</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">These five writings attributed to the Apostolic Fathers are not currently considered canonical in any biblical tradition, though they are more highly regarded by some more than others.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> Nonetheless, their early authorship and inclusion in ancient biblical codices, as well as their acceptance to varying degrees by various early authorities, requires them to be treated as foundational literature for Christianity as a whole.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag may use weasel words or too-vague attribution. (February 2016)">according to whom?</span></a></i>&#93;</sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup></span>

(February 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup></span>

</ol></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Book_order">Book order</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Testament&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Book order">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The order in which the books of the New Testament appear differs between some collections and ecclesiastical traditions. In the Latin West, prior to the <a href="/wiki/Vulgate" title="Vulgate">Vulgate</a> (an early 5th-century Latin version of the Bible), the four Gospels were arranged in the following order: Matthew, John, Luke, and Mark.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;note 4&#93;</a></sup> The Syriac <a href="/wiki/Peshitta" title="Peshitta">Peshitta</a> places the major Catholic epistles (James, 1 Peter, and 1 John) immediately after Acts and before the Pauline epistles.
</p><p>The order of an early edition of the letters of Paul is based on the size of the letters: longest to shortest, though keeping 1 and 2 Corinthians and 1 and 2 Thessalonians together. The Pastoral epistles were apparently not part of the <i>Corpus Paulinum</i> in which this order originated and were later inserted after 2 Thessalonians and before Philemon. Hebrews was variously incorporated into the <i>Corpus Paulinum</i> either after 2 Thessalonians, after Philemon (i.e. at the very end), or after Romans.
</p><p>The New Testament of the 16th-century <a href="/wiki/Luther_Bible" title="Luther Bible">Luther Bible</a> continues, to this day, to place Hebrews, James, Jude, and the Apocalypse last. This reflects the thoughts of the Reformer Martin Luther <a href="/wiki/Luther%27s_canon" title="Luther&#39;s canon">on the canonicity of these books</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;note 5&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Apocrypha">Apocrypha</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Testament&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Apocrypha">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/New_Testament_apocrypha" title="New Testament apocrypha">New Testament apocrypha</a></div>
<p>The books that eventually found a permanent place in the New Testament were not the only works of Christian literature produced in the earliest Christian centuries. The long process of <a href="/wiki/Development_of_the_New_Testament_canon" title="Development of the New Testament canon">canonization</a> began early, sometimes with tacit reception of traditional texts, sometimes with explicit selection or rejection of particular texts as either acceptable or unacceptable for use in a given context (e.g., not all texts that were acceptable for private use were considered appropriate for use in the <a href="/wiki/Liturgy" title="Liturgy">liturgy</a>).
</p><p>Over the course of history, those works of early Christian literature that survived but that did not become part of the New Testament have been variously grouped by theologians and scholars. Drawing upon, though redefining, an older term used in <a href="/wiki/Early_Christianity" title="Early Christianity">early Christianity</a> and among Protestants when referring to those books found in the Christian <a href="/wiki/Old_Testament" title="Old Testament">Old Testament</a> although not in the <a href="/wiki/Tanakh" class="mw-redirect" title="Tanakh">Jewish Bible</a>, modern scholars began to refer to these works of early Christian literature not included in the New Testament as "apocryphal", by which was meant non-canonical.
</p><p>Collected editions of these works were then referred to as the "<a href="/wiki/New_Testament_apocrypha" title="New Testament apocrypha">New Testament apocrypha</a>". Typically excluded from such published collections are the following groups of works: <a href="/wiki/Apostolic_Fathers" title="Apostolic Fathers">The Apostolic Fathers</a>, the 2nd-century Christian apologists, <a href="/wiki/Alexandrian_school" title="Alexandrian school">the Alexandrians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tertullian" title="Tertullian">Tertullian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Methodius_of_Olympus" title="Methodius of Olympus">Methodius of Olympus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Novatian" title="Novatian">Novatian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cyprian" title="Cyprian">Cyprian</a>, martyrdoms, and <a href="/wiki/Desert_Fathers" title="Desert Fathers">the Desert Fathers</a>. Almost all other Christian literature from the period, and sometimes including works composed well into <a href="/wiki/Late_Antiquity" class="mw-redirect" title="Late Antiquity">Late Antiquity</a>, are relegated to the so-called New Testament apocrypha.
</p><p>Although not considered to be inspired by God,  these "apocryphal" works were produced in the same ancient context and often using the same language as those books that would eventually form the New Testament. Some of these later works are dependent (either directly or indirectly) upon books that would later come to be in the New Testament or upon the ideas expressed in them. There is even an example of a <a href="/wiki/Pseudepigraph" class="mw-redirect" title="Pseudepigraph">pseudepigraphical</a> letter composed under the guise of a presumably lost letter of the Apostle Paul, the <a href="/wiki/Epistle_to_the_Laodiceans" title="Epistle to the Laodiceans">Epistle to the Laodiceans</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Authors">Authors</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Testament&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Authors">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Authorship_of_the_Bible" title="Authorship of the Bible">Authorship of the Bible</a></div>
<p>The books of the New Testament were all or nearly all written by <a href="/wiki/Jewish_Christians" class="mw-redirect" title="Jewish Christians">Jewish Christians</a>—that is, Jewish disciples of Christ, who lived in the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman Empire</a>, and under <a href="/wiki/Judea_(Roman_province)" title="Judea (Roman province)">Roman occupation</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> Luke, who wrote the Gospel of Luke and the Book of Acts, is frequently thought of as an exception; scholars are divided as to whether Luke was a Gentile or a <a href="/wiki/Hellenistic_Judaism" title="Hellenistic Judaism">Hellenistic Jew</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Strelan2013_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Strelan2013-57">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> A few scholars identify the author of the Gospel of Mark as probably a Gentile, and similarly for the Gospel of Matthew, though most assert Jewish-Christian authorship.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="The material near this tag needs to be fact-checked with the cited source(s). (February 2016)">verification needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Gospels">Gospels</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Testament&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Gospels">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Synoptic_Gospels" title="Synoptic Gospels">Synoptic Gospels</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Evangelist_Matthew_Inspired_by_an_Angel.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/The_Evangelist_Matthew_Inspired_by_an_Angel.jpg/220px-The_Evangelist_Matthew_Inspired_by_an_Angel.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="281" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/The_Evangelist_Matthew_Inspired_by_an_Angel.jpg/330px-The_Evangelist_Matthew_Inspired_by_an_Angel.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/The_Evangelist_Matthew_Inspired_by_an_Angel.jpg/440px-The_Evangelist_Matthew_Inspired_by_an_Angel.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1210" data-file-height="1545" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Evangelist_Matthew_Inspired_by_an_Angel.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Evangelist Mathäus und der Engel</i>, by <a href="/wiki/Rembrandt" title="Rembrandt">Rembrandt</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>According to the large majority of <a href="/wiki/Biblical_criticism" title="Biblical criticism">critical scholars</a>, none of the authors of the Gospels were eyewitnesses or even explicitly claimed to be eyewitnesses.<sup id="cite_ref-lost_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lost-61">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Carolina2004_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Carolina2004-62">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-iuda_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-iuda-63">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Bart_D._Ehrman" title="Bart D. Ehrman">Bart D. Ehrman</a> of the <a href="/wiki/University_of_North_Carolina" title="University of North Carolina">University of North Carolina</a> has argued for a scholarly consensus that many New Testament books were not written by the individuals whose names are attached to them.<sup id="cite_ref-iuda_63-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-iuda-63">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> He further argues that names were not ascribed to the gospels until around 185 AD.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> Other scholars concur.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Nickle2001_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nickle2001-68">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Witherington2004_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Witherington2004-69">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> Many scholars believe that that none of the gospels were written in the region of <a href="/wiki/Palestine_(region)" title="Palestine (region)">Palestine</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Christian_tradition" title="Christian tradition">Christian tradition</a> identifies <a href="/wiki/John_the_Apostle" title="John the Apostle">John the Apostle</a> with <a href="/wiki/John_the_Evangelist" title="John the Evangelist">John the Evangelist</a>, the supposed author of the <a href="/wiki/Gospel_of_John" title="Gospel of John">Gospel of John</a>. Traditionalists tend to support the idea that the writer of the Gospel of John himself claimed to be an eyewitness in their commentaries of <a href="/wiki/John_21" title="John 21">John 21</a>:24 and therefore the gospel was written by an eyewitness;<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Henry_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Henry-72">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> however, this idea is rejected by the majority of modern scholars.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Most scholars hold to the <a href="/wiki/Two-source_hypothesis" title="Two-source hypothesis">two-source hypothesis</a>, which posits that the <a href="/wiki/Marcan_priority" title="Marcan priority">Gospel of Mark was the first gospel to be written</a>. On this view, the authors of the <a href="/wiki/Gospel_of_Matthew" title="Gospel of Matthew">Gospel of Matthew</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Gospel_of_Luke" title="Gospel of Luke">Gospel of Luke</a> used as sources the <a href="/wiki/Gospel_of_Mark" title="Gospel of Mark">Gospel of Mark</a> and a hypothetical <a href="/wiki/Q_document" class="mw-redirect" title="Q document">Q document</a> to write their individual gospel accounts.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> These three gospels are called the <a href="/wiki/Synoptic_Gospels" title="Synoptic Gospels">Synoptic Gospels</a>, because they include many of the same stories, often in the same sequence, and sometimes in exactly the same wording. Scholars agree that the Gospel of John was written last, by using a different tradition and body of testimony. In addition, most scholars agree that the author of Luke also wrote the <a href="/wiki/Acts_of_the_Apostles" title="Acts of the Apostles">Acts of the Apostles</a>. Scholars hold that these books constituted two halves of a single work, <a href="/wiki/Luke-Acts" class="mw-redirect" title="Luke-Acts">Luke-Acts</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="Scholars not named. (July 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>All four gospels and the Acts of the Apostles are <a href="/wiki/Anonymous_work" title="Anonymous work">anonymous works</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Harris_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Harris-79">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> The Gospel of John claims to be based on eyewitness testimony from the <a href="/wiki/Disciple_whom_Jesus_loved" title="Disciple whom Jesus loved">Disciple whom Jesus loved</a>, but never names this character.<sup id="cite_ref-Harris_John_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Harris_John-80">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Acts">Acts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Testament&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Acts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Authorship_of_Luke%E2%80%93Acts" title="Authorship of Luke–Acts">Authorship of Luke–Acts</a></div>
<p>The Gospel of Luke and the Acts of the Apostles were both written by the same author, and are thus referred to as the Lucan texts.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> The most direct evidence comes from the prefaces of each book; both were addressed to <a href="/wiki/Theophilus_(Biblical)" class="mw-redirect" title="Theophilus (Biblical)">Theophilus</a>, and the preface to the Acts of the Apostles references "my former book" about the ministry of Jesus.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> Furthermore, there are linguistic and theological similarities between the two works, suggesting that they have a common author.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pauline_epistles">Pauline epistles</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Testament&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Pauline epistles">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Authorship_of_the_Pauline_epistles" title="Authorship of the Pauline epistles">Authorship of the Pauline epistles</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:File%22-Saint_Paul_Writing_His_Epistles%22_by_Valentin_de_Boulogne.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/File%22-Saint_Paul_Writing_His_Epistles%22_by_Valentin_de_Boulogne.jpg/220px-File%22-Saint_Paul_Writing_His_Epistles%22_by_Valentin_de_Boulogne.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="163" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/File%22-Saint_Paul_Writing_His_Epistles%22_by_Valentin_de_Boulogne.jpg/330px-File%22-Saint_Paul_Writing_His_Epistles%22_by_Valentin_de_Boulogne.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/File%22-Saint_Paul_Writing_His_Epistles%22_by_Valentin_de_Boulogne.jpg/440px-File%22-Saint_Paul_Writing_His_Epistles%22_by_Valentin_de_Boulogne.jpg 2x" data-file-width="599" data-file-height="445" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:File%22-Saint_Paul_Writing_His_Epistles%22_by_Valentin_de_Boulogne.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Saint Paul Writing His Epistles</i>, 17th-century painting. Most scholars think Paul actually dictated his letters to a secretary.</div></div></div>
<p>The Pauline epistles are the thirteen books in the New Testament traditionally attributed to <a href="/wiki/Paul_of_Tarsus" class="mw-redirect" title="Paul of Tarsus">Paul of Tarsus</a>. The anonymous Epistle to the Hebrews is, despite unlikely Pauline authorship, often functionally grouped with these thirteen to form a corpus of fourteen "Pauline" epistles.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;note 6&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Seven letters are generally classified as "undisputed", expressing contemporary scholarly near consensus that they are the work of Paul: Romans, 1 Corinthians, 2 Corinthians, Galatians, Philippians, 1 Thessalonians and Philemon. Six additional letters bearing Paul's name do not currently enjoy the same academic consensus: Ephesians, Colossians, 2 Thessalonians, 1 Timothy, 2 Timothy and Titus.<sup id="cite_ref-Lock,_Meinertz_p.622_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lock,_Meinertz_p.622-89">&#91;note 7&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>While many scholars uphold the traditional view, some question whether the first three, called the "Deutero-Pauline Epistles", are authentic letters of Paul. As for the latter three, the "Pastoral epistles", some scholars uphold the traditional view of these as the genuine writings of the Apostle Paul;<sup id="cite_ref-Lock,_Meinertz_p.622_89-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lock,_Meinertz_p.622-89">&#91;note 7&#93;</a></sup> most, however, regard them as <a href="/wiki/Pseudepigraphy" class="mw-redirect" title="Pseudepigraphy">pseudepigrapha</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>One might refer to the <a href="/wiki/Epistle_to_the_Laodiceans" title="Epistle to the Laodiceans">Epistle to the Laodiceans</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Third_Epistle_to_the_Corinthians" title="Third Epistle to the Corinthians">Third Epistle to the Corinthians</a> as examples of works identified as pseudonymous. Since the early centuries of the church, there has been debate concerning the authorship of the anonymous Epistle to the Hebrews, and contemporary scholars generally reject Pauline authorship.<sup id="cite_ref-Ehrman_2004_29-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ehrman_2004-29">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The epistles all share common themes, emphasis, vocabulary and style; they exhibit a uniformity of doctrine concerning the <a href="/wiki/Ten_Commandments" title="Ten Commandments">Mosaic Law</a>, Jesus, faith, and various other issues. All of these letters easily fit into the chronology of Paul's journeys depicted in Acts of the Apostles.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_epistles">Other epistles</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Testament&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Other epistles">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The author of the <a href="/wiki/Epistle_of_James" title="Epistle of James">Epistle of James</a> identifies himself in the opening verse as "James, a servant of <a href="/wiki/God" title="God">God</a> and of the Lord <a href="/wiki/Jesus" title="Jesus">Jesus</a> Christ". From the middle of the 3rd century, <a href="/wiki/Patristic" class="mw-redirect" title="Patristic">patristic</a> authors cited the <i>Epistle</i> as written by <a href="/wiki/James_the_Just" class="mw-redirect" title="James the Just">James the Just</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> Ancient and modern scholars have always been divided on the issue of authorship. Many consider the epistle to be written in the late 1st or early 2nd centuries.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup>
