</p><p>The author of the <a href="/wiki/First_Epistle_of_Peter" title="First Epistle of Peter">First Epistle of Peter</a> identifies himself in the opening verse as "Peter, an <a href="/wiki/Apostle_(Christian)" class="mw-redirect" title="Apostle (Christian)">apostle</a> of Jesus Christ", and the view that the epistle was written by St. Peter is attested to by a number of <a href="/wiki/Church_Fathers" title="Church Fathers">Church Fathers</a>: <a href="/wiki/Irenaeus" title="Irenaeus">Irenaeus</a> (140–203), <a href="/wiki/Tertullian" title="Tertullian">Tertullian</a> (150–222), <a href="/wiki/Clement_of_Alexandria" title="Clement of Alexandria">Clement of Alexandria</a> (155–215) and <a href="/wiki/Origen_of_Alexandria" class="mw-redirect" title="Origen of Alexandria">Origen of Alexandria</a> (185–253). Unlike <a href="/wiki/The_Second_Epistle_of_Peter" class="mw-redirect" title="The Second Epistle of Peter">The Second Epistle of Peter</a>, the authorship of which was debated in antiquity, there was little debate about Peter's authorship of this first epistle until the 18th century. Although 2 Peter internally purports to be a work of the apostle, many biblical scholars have concluded that Peter is not the author.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> For an early date and (usually) for a defense of the Apostle Peter's authorship see Kruger,<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> Zahn,<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> Spitta,<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> Bigg,<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> and Green.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Epistle of Jude title is written as follows: "Jude, a servant of Jesus Christ and a brother of James" (<a href="/wiki/New_Revised_Standard_Version" title="New Revised Standard Version">NRSV</a>). The debate has continued over the author's identity as the apostle, the brother of Jesus, both, or neither.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Johannine_works">Johannine works</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Testament&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Johannine works">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Authorship_of_the_Johannine_works" title="Authorship of the Johannine works">Authorship of the Johannine works</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/First_Epistle_of_John" title="First Epistle of John">First Epistle of John</a> is traditionally held to have been composed by <a href="/wiki/John_the_Apostle" title="John the Apostle">John the Apostle</a> (the author of the <a href="/wiki/Gospel_of_John" title="Gospel of John">Gospel of John</a>) when the writer was in advanced age. The epistle's content, language and conceptual style indicate that it may have had the same author as the Gospel of John, 2 John and 3 John.<sup id="cite_ref-Harris_1John_100-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Harris_1John-100">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Eusebius" title="Eusebius">Eusebius</a> claimed that the author of 2nd and 3rd John was not <a href="/wiki/John_the_Apostle" title="John the Apostle">John the Apostle</a>, but an "elder John" which refers either to the apostle at an advanced age or a hypothetical second individual ("<a href="/wiki/John_the_Elder" class="mw-redirect" title="John the Elder">John the Elder</a>").<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> Scholars today are divided on the issue.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Revelation">Revelation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Testament&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Revelation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The author of the <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Revelation" title="Book of Revelation">Book of Revelation</a> identifies himself several times as "John".<sup class="nourlexpansion reference"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=Rev.+1%3A1%2C+4%2C+9%3B+22%3A8&amp;version=NRSV">&#91;Rev.&#160;1:1,&#160;4,&#160;9;&#160;22:8&#93;</a></sup> and states that he was on <a href="/wiki/Patmos" title="Patmos">Patmos</a> when he received his first vision.<sup class="nourlexpansion reference"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=Rev.+1%3A9%3B+4%3A1%E2%80%932&amp;version=NRSV">&#91;Rev.&#160;1:9;&#160;4:1–2&#93;</a></sup> As a result, the author is sometimes referred to as <a href="/wiki/John_of_Patmos" title="John of Patmos">John of Patmos</a>. The author has traditionally been identified with <a href="/wiki/John_the_Apostle" title="John the Apostle">John the Apostle</a> to whom the <a href="/wiki/Gospel_of_John" title="Gospel of John">Gospel</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Epistles_of_John" class="mw-redirect" title="Epistles of John">epistles of John</a> were attributed. It was believed that he was exiled to the island of Patmos during the reign of the <a href="/wiki/Roman_emperor" title="Roman emperor">Roman emperor</a> <a href="/wiki/Domitian" title="Domitian">Domitian</a>, and there wrote Revelation. <a href="/wiki/Justin_Martyr" title="Justin Martyr">Justin Martyr</a> (c. 100–165 AD) who was acquainted with <a href="/wiki/Polycarp" title="Polycarp">Polycarp</a>, who had been mentored by John, makes a possible allusion to this book, and credits John as the source.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Irenaeus" title="Irenaeus">Irenaeus</a> (c. 115–202) assumes it as a conceded point. According to the <i>Zondervan Pictorial Encyclopedia of the Bible</i>, modern scholars are divided between the apostolic view and several alternative hypotheses put forth in the last hundred years or so.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ben_Witherington" class="mw-redirect" title="Ben Witherington">Ben Witherington</a> points out that linguistic evidence makes it unlikely that the books were written by the same person.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Dating_the_New_Testament">Dating the New Testament</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Testament&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Dating the New Testament">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Dating_the_Bible#Table_IV:_New_Testament" title="Dating the Bible">Dating the Bible §&#160;Table IV: New Testament</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="External_evidence">External evidence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Testament&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: External evidence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The earliest <a href="/wiki/Biblical_manuscript" title="Biblical manuscript">manuscripts</a> of New Testament books date from the late second to early third centuries (although see <a href="/wiki/Rylands_Library_Papyrus_P52" title="Rylands Library Papyrus P52">Papyrus 52</a> for a possible exception).<sup id="cite_ref-Ehrman_2004_29-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ehrman_2004-29">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>479-480</span></sup> These manuscripts place a clear <a href="/wiki/Terminus_post_quem" title="Terminus post quem">upper limit</a> on the dating of New Testament texts. Explicit references to NT books in extra-biblical documents can push this upper limit down a bit further. <a href="/wiki/Irenaeus" title="Irenaeus">Irenaeus</a> of Lyon names and quotes from most of the books in the New Testament in his book <a href="/wiki/Against_Heresies_(Irenaeus)" title="Against Heresies (Irenaeus)"><i>Against Heresies</i></a>, written around 180 AD. The <a href="/wiki/Epistle_of_Polycarp_to_the_Philippians" title="Epistle of Polycarp to the Philippians">Epistle of Polycarp to the Philippians</a>, written some time between 110 and <a href="/wiki/Polycarp" title="Polycarp">Polycarp's death</a> in 155-167 AD, quotes or alludes to most New Testament texts. Biblical scholar <a href="/wiki/David_Trobisch" title="David Trobisch">David Trobisch</a> concludes in his survey of the evidence that no book of the New Testament could have been written later than 167 AD, the latest date for the martyrdom of <a href="/wiki/Polycarp" title="Polycarp">Polycarp</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-trobisch-2007_5-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-trobisch-2007-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Internal_evidence">Internal evidence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Testament&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Internal evidence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Literary analysis of the New Testament texts themselves can be used to date many of the books of the New Testament to the mid- to late first century. The earliest works of the New Testament are the letters of the <a href="/wiki/Paul_the_Apostle" title="Paul the Apostle">Apostle Paul</a>. It can be determined that <a href="/wiki/First_Epistle_to_the_Thessalonians" title="First Epistle to the Thessalonians">1 Thessalonians</a> is likely the earliest of these letters, written around 52 AD.<sup id="cite_ref-Brown_105-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-105">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Language">Language</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Testament&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Language">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Language_of_the_New_Testament" title="Language of the New Testament">Language of the New Testament</a></div>
<p>The major languages spoken by both Jews and Greeks in the <a href="/wiki/Holy_Land" title="Holy Land">Holy Land</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Cultural_and_historical_background_of_Jesus" class="mw-redirect" title="Cultural and historical background of Jesus">time of Jesus</a> were <a href="/wiki/Aramaic_of_Jesus" class="mw-redirect" title="Aramaic of Jesus">Aramaic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Koine_Greek" title="Koine Greek">Koine Greek</a>, and also a colloquial dialect of <a href="/wiki/Mishnaic_Hebrew" title="Mishnaic Hebrew">Mishnaic Hebrew</a>. It is generally agreed by most scholars that the <a href="/wiki/Historical_Jesus" title="Historical Jesus">historical Jesus</a> primarily spoke <a href="/wiki/Aramaic_of_Jesus" class="mw-redirect" title="Aramaic of Jesus">Aramaic</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> perhaps also some <a href="/wiki/Hebrew_language" title="Hebrew language">Hebrew</a> and <a href="/wiki/Koine_Greek" title="Koine Greek">Koine Greek</a>. The majority view is that all of the books that would eventually form the New Testament were written in the Koine Greek language.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Early_centers_of_Christianity" title="Early centers of Christianity">As Christianity spread</a>, these books were later translated into other languages, most notably, <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Syriac_language" title="Syriac language">Syriac</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Coptic_language" title="Coptic language">Egyptian Coptic</a>. However, some of the <a href="/wiki/Church_Fathers" title="Church Fathers">Church Fathers</a><sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> imply or claim that Matthew was originally written in <a href="/wiki/Hebrew_Gospel_of_Matthew" class="mw-redirect" title="Hebrew Gospel of Matthew">Hebrew</a> or <a href="/wiki/Aramaic_Matthew" class="mw-redirect" title="Aramaic Matthew">Aramaic</a>, and then soon after was written in Koine Greek. Nevertheless, the Gospel of Matthew known today was composed in Greek and is neither directly dependent upon nor a translation of a text in a <a href="/wiki/Semitic_languages" title="Semitic languages">Semitic language</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Development_of_the_New_Testament_canon">Development of the New Testament canon</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Testament&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Development of the New Testament canon">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Development_of_the_New_Testament_canon" title="Development of the New Testament canon">Development of the New Testament canon</a></div>
<p>The process of canonization of the New Testament was complex and lengthy. In the initial centuries of <a href="/wiki/Early_Christianity" title="Early Christianity">early Christianity</a>, there were many books widely considered by the church to be inspired, but there was no single formally recognized New Testament canon.<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> The process was characterized by a compilation of books that <a href="/wiki/Sacred_tradition" title="Sacred tradition">apostolic tradition</a> considered authoritative in worship and teaching, relevant to the historical situations in which they lived, and consonant with the Old Testament.<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> Writings attributed to the apostles circulated among the <a href="/wiki/Early_centers_of_Christianity" title="Early centers of Christianity">earliest Christian communities</a> and the Pauline epistles were circulating, perhaps in collected forms, by the end of the <a href="/wiki/Christianity_in_the_1st_century" title="Christianity in the 1st century">1st century AD</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>One of the earliest attempts at solidifying a canon was made by <a href="/wiki/Marcion" class="mw-redirect" title="Marcion">Marcion</a>, <i>circa</i> 140 AD, who accepted only a modified version of Luke (the <a href="/wiki/Gospel_of_Marcion" title="Gospel of Marcion">Gospel of Marcion</a>) and ten of Paul's letters, while rejecting the Old Testament entirely. His canon was largely rejected by other groups of Christians, notably the <a href="/wiki/Proto-orthodox_Christianity" title="Proto-orthodox Christianity">proto-orthodox Christians</a>, as was his theology, <a href="/wiki/Marcionism" title="Marcionism">Marcionism</a>. <a href="/wiki/Adolf_von_Harnack" title="Adolf von Harnack">Adolf von Harnack</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> John Knox,<sup id="cite_ref-knox_115-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-knox-115">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/David_Trobisch" title="David Trobisch">David Trobisch</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-trobisch-2007_5-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-trobisch-2007-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> among other scholars, have argued that the church formulated its New Testament canon partially in response to the challenge posed by Marcion.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Polycarp" title="Polycarp">Polycarp</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Irenaeus" title="Irenaeus">Irenaeus</a><sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Tertullian" title="Tertullian">Tertullian</a><sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup> held the <a href="/wiki/Pauline_epistles" title="Pauline epistles">epistles of Paul</a> to be divinely inspired "scripture." Other books were held in high esteem but were gradually relegated to the status of <a href="/wiki/New_Testament_apocrypha" title="New Testament apocrypha">New Testament apocrypha</a>. Justin Martyr, in the mid <a href="/wiki/Christianity_in_the_2nd_century" title="Christianity in the 2nd century">2nd century</a>, mentions "memoirs of the apostles" as being read on Sunday alongside the <a href="/wiki/Nevi%27im" title="Nevi&#39;im">"writings of the prophets"</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_119-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-119">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Muratorian_fragment" title="Muratorian fragment">Muratorian fragment</a>, dated at between 170 and as late as the end of the 4th century (according to the <a href="/wiki/Anchor_Bible_Series#Anchor_Bible_Dictionary" title="Anchor Bible Series">Anchor Bible Dictionary</a>), may be the earliest known New Testament canon attributed to mainstream Christianity. It is similar, but not identical, to the modern New Testament canon.
</p><p>The oldest clear endorsement of Matthew, Mark, Luke, and John being the only legitimate gospels was written <i>circa</i> 180 AD. A four gospel canon (the <i>Tetramorph</i>) was asserted by Irenaeus, who refers to it directly<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-AHIII8_121-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AHIII8-121">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> in his <a href="/wiki/Polemic" title="Polemic">polemic</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Against_Heresies_(Irenaeus)" title="Against Heresies (Irenaeus)">Against Heresies</a></i>:
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886047036"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>"<b>It is not possible that the gospels can be either more or fewer in number than they are.</b> For, since there are four zones of the world in which we live, and four principal winds, while the church is scattered throughout all the world, and the 'pillar and ground' of the church is the gospel and the spirit of life; it is fitting that she should have four pillars, breathing out immortality on every side, and vivifying men afresh." (emphasis added)<sup id="cite_ref-AHIII8_121-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AHIII8-121">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup></p><div class="templatequotecite">—&#8201;<cite><a href="/wiki/Irenaeus" title="Irenaeus">Irenaeus of Lyon</a></cite></div>
</blockquote>
<p>The books considered to be authoritative by Irenaeus included the four gospels and many of the letters of Paul, although, based on the arguments Irenaeus made in support of only four authentic gospels, some interpreters deduce that the fourfold Gospel must have still been a novelty in Irenaeus's time.<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Origen_.283rd_century.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Origen_(3rd_century)">Origen (3rd century)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Testament&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Origen (3rd century)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>By the early 200s, <a href="/wiki/Origen" title="Origen">Origen</a> may have been using the same twenty-seven books as in the Catholic New Testament canon, though there were still disputes over the canonicity of the Letter to the Hebrews, Epistle of James, II Peter, II John and III John and the Book of Revelation,<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup> known as the <a href="/wiki/Antilegomena" title="Antilegomena">Antilegomena</a>. Likewise, the <a href="/wiki/Muratorian_fragment" title="Muratorian fragment">Muratorian fragment</a> is evidence that, perhaps as early as 200, there existed a set of Christian writings somewhat similar to the twenty-seven book NT canon, which included four gospels and argued against objections to them.<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup> Thus, while there was a good measure of debate in the <a href="/wiki/Early_Church" class="mw-redirect" title="Early Church">Early Church</a> over the New Testament canon, the major writings are claimed to have been accepted by almost all Christians by the middle of the <a href="/wiki/Christianity_in_the_3rd_century" title="Christianity in the 3rd century">3rd century</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Origen was largely responsible for the collection of usage information regarding the texts that became the New Testament. The information used to create the late-4th-century <a href="/wiki/Easter_Letter" class="mw-redirect" title="Easter Letter">Easter Letter</a>, which declared accepted Christian writings, was probably based on the <i>Ecclesiastical History</i> [HE] of <a href="/wiki/Eusebius_of_Caesarea" class="mw-redirect" title="Eusebius of Caesarea">Eusebius of Caesarea</a>, wherein he uses the information passed on to him by Origen to create both his list at HE 3:25 and Origen's list at HE 6:25. Eusebius got his information about what texts were then accepted and what were then <a href="/wiki/Antilegomena" title="Antilegomena">disputed</a>, by the <a href="/wiki/Early_centers_of_Christianity" title="Early centers of Christianity">third-century churches throughout the known world</a>, a great deal of which Origen knew of firsthand from his extensive travels, from the library and writings of Origen.<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In fact, Origen would have possibly included in his list of "inspired writings" other texts kept out by the likes of Eusebius—including the <a href="/wiki/Epistle_of_Barnabas" title="Epistle of Barnabas">Epistle of Barnabas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shepherd_of_Hermas" class="mw-redirect" title="Shepherd of Hermas">Shepherd of Hermas</a>, and <a href="/wiki/1_Clement" class="mw-redirect" title="1 Clement">1 Clement</a>. Notwithstanding these facts, "Origen is not the originator of the idea of biblical canon, but he certainly gives the philosophical and literary-interpretative underpinnings for the whole notion."<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Eusebius.27s_Ecclesiastical_History"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Eusebius's_Ecclesiastical_History">Eusebius's Ecclesiastical History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Testament&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Eusebius&#039;s Ecclesiastical History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Eusebius" title="Eusebius">Eusebius</a>, <i>circa</i> 300, gave a detailed list of New Testament writings in his <i>Ecclesiastical History</i> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.ccel.org/fathers2/NPNF2-01/Npnf2-01-08.htm#P1497_696002">Book 3</a>, Chapter XXV:
</p>
<dl><dd>"1... First then must be put the holy quaternion of the gospels; following them the Acts of the Apostles... the epistles of Paul... the epistle of John... the epistle of Peter... After them is to be placed, if it really seem proper, the Book of Revelation, concerning which we shall give the different opinions at the proper time. These then belong among the accepted writings."</dd></dl>
<dl><dd>"3 Among the disputed writings, which are nevertheless recognized by many, are extant the so-called epistle of James and that of Jude, also the second epistle of Peter, and those that are called the second and third of John, whether they belong to the evangelist or to another person of the same name. Among the rejected [Kirsopp Lake translation: "not genuine"] writings must be reckoned also the <a href="/wiki/Acts_of_Paul" title="Acts of Paul">Acts of Paul</a>, and the so-called <a href="/wiki/Shepherd_of_Hermas" class="mw-redirect" title="Shepherd of Hermas">Shepherd</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Apocalypse_of_Peter" title="Apocalypse of Peter">Apocalypse of Peter</a>, and in addition to these the extant <a href="/wiki/Epistle_of_Barnabas" title="Epistle of Barnabas">epistle of Barnabas</a>, and the so-called <a href="/wiki/Didache" title="Didache">Teachings of the Apostles</a>; and besides, as I said, the <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Revelation" title="Book of Revelation">Apocalypse of John</a>, if it seem proper, which some, as I said, reject, but which others class with the accepted books. And among these some have placed also the <a href="/wiki/Gospel_of_the_Hebrews" title="Gospel of the Hebrews">Gospel according to the Hebrews</a>... And all these may be reckoned among the disputed books."</dd></dl>
<dl><dd>"6... such books as the <a href="/wiki/Gospel_of_Peter" title="Gospel of Peter">Gospels of Peter</a>, of <a href="/wiki/Gospel_of_Thomas" title="Gospel of Thomas">Thomas</a>, of <a href="/wiki/Gospel_of_Matthias" title="Gospel of Matthias">Matthias</a>, or of any others besides them, and the <a href="/wiki/Acts_of_Andrew" title="Acts of Andrew">Acts of Andrew</a> and <a href="/wiki/Acts_of_John" title="Acts of John">John</a> and the other apostles... they clearly show themselves to be the fictions of heretics. Wherefore they are not to be placed even among the rejected writings, but are all of them to be cast aside as absurd and impious."</dd></dl>
<p>The Book of Revelation is counted as both accepted (Kirsopp Lake translation: "Recognized") and disputed, which has caused some confusion over what exactly Eusebius meant by doing so. From other writings of the church fathers, it was disputed with several canon lists rejecting its canonicity. EH 3.3.5 adds further detail on Paul: "Paul's fourteen epistles are well known and undisputed. It is not indeed right to overlook the fact that some have rejected the Epistle to the Hebrews, saying that it is disputed by the church of Rome, on the ground that it was not written by Paul." EH 4.29.6 mentions the <a href="/wiki/Diatessaron" title="Diatessaron">Diatessaron</a>: "But their original founder, Tatian, formed a certain combination and collection of the gospels, I know not how, to which he gave the title Diatessaron, and which is still in the hands of some. But they say that he ventured to paraphrase certain words of the apostle Paul, in order to improve their style."
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="4th_century_and_later">4th century and later</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Testament&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: 4th century and later">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In his Easter letter of 367, <a href="/wiki/Athanasius" class="mw-redirect" title="Athanasius">Athanasius</a>, Bishop of Alexandria, gave a list of the books that would become the twenty-seven-book NT canon,<sup id="cite_ref-LindbergCarter_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LindbergCarter-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> and he used the word "canonized" (<i>kanonizomena</i>) in regards to them.<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup> The first council that accepted the present canon of the New Testament may have been the <a href="/wiki/Synod_of_Hippo" title="Synod of Hippo">Synod of Hippo Regius</a> in North Africa (393 AD); the acts of this council, however, are lost. A brief summary of the acts was read at and accepted by the <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Carthage_(397)" class="mw-redirect" title="Council of Carthage (397)">Council of Carthage (397)</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Carthage_(419)" class="mw-redirect" title="Council of Carthage (419)">Council of Carthage (419)</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-McDonaldD2_129-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McDonaldD2-129">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup> These councils were under the authority of <a href="/wiki/Augustine_of_Hippo" title="Augustine of Hippo">St. Augustine</a>, who regarded the canon as already closed.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson320_130-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson320-130">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Pope_Damasus_I" title="Pope Damasus I">Pope Damasus I</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Rome" title="Council of Rome">Council of Rome</a> in 382, if the <i><a href="/wiki/Decretum_Gelasianum" title="Decretum Gelasianum">Decretum Gelasianum</a></i> is correctly associated with it, issued a biblical canon identical to that mentioned above,<sup id="cite_ref-LindbergCarter_1-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LindbergCarter-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> or, if not, the list is at least a 6th-century compilation.<sup id="cite_ref-Bruce_1988_234_133-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bruce_1988_234-133">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup> Likewise, Damasus' commissioning of the Latin <a href="/wiki/Vulgate" title="Vulgate">Vulgate</a> edition of the Bible, c. 383, was instrumental in the fixation of the canon in the West.<sup id="cite_ref-Bruce_1988_225_134-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bruce_1988_225-134">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup> In <i>c.</i> 405, <a href="/wiki/Pope_Innocent_I" title="Pope Innocent I">Pope Innocent I</a> sent a list of the sacred books to a Gallic bishop, <a href="/wiki/Exuperius" title="Exuperius">Exsuperius of Toulouse</a>. Christian scholars assert that, when these <a href="/wiki/Bishops" class="mw-redirect" title="Bishops">bishops</a> and councils spoke on the matter, however, they were not defining something new but instead "were ratifying what had already become the mind of the Church."<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson320_130-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson320-130">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Metzger_1987_237–238_135-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Metzger_1987_237–238-135">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Bruce_136-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bruce-136">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The New Testament canon as it is now was first listed by <a href="/wiki/Athanasius_of_Alexandria" title="Athanasius of Alexandria">St. Athanasius, Bishop of Alexandria</a>, in 367, in a letter written to his churches in Egypt, <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.ccel.org/fathers2/NPNF2-04/Npnf2-04-93.htm">Festal Letter 39</a>. Also cited is the <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Rome" title="Council of Rome">Council of Rome</a>, but not without controversy. That canon gained wider and wider recognition until it was accepted at the <a href="/wiki/Third_Council_of_Carthage" class="mw-redirect" title="Third Council of Carthage">Third Council of Carthage</a> in 397 and 419.<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">&#91;note 8&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Even this council did not settle the matter, however. Certain books, referred to as <a href="/wiki/Antilegomena" title="Antilegomena">Antilegomena</a>, continued to be questioned, especially <a href="/wiki/Epistle_of_James" title="Epistle of James">James</a> and <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Revelation" title="Book of Revelation">Revelation</a>. Even as late as the 16th century, the Reformer <a href="/wiki/Martin_Luther" title="Martin Luther">Martin Luther</a> questioned (but in the end did not reject) the <a href="/wiki/Epistle_of_James" title="Epistle of James">Epistle of James</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Epistle_of_Jude" title="Epistle of Jude">Epistle of Jude</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Epistle_to_the_Hebrews" title="Epistle to the Hebrews">Epistle to the Hebrews</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Revelation" title="Book of Revelation">Book of Revelation</a>. To this day, German-language <a href="/wiki/Luther_Bible" title="Luther Bible">Luther Bibles</a> are printed with these four books at the end of the canon, rather than in their traditional order as in other editions of the Bible.
</p><p>In light of this questioning of the canon of Scripture by Protestants in the 16th century, the (Roman Catholic) <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Trent" title="Council of Trent">Council of Trent</a> reaffirmed the traditional western canon (i.e., the canon accepted at the 4th-century <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Rome" title="Council of Rome">Council of Rome</a> and <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Carthage" class="mw-redirect" title="Council of Carthage">Council of Carthage</a>), thus making the <a href="/wiki/Canon_of_Trent" title="Canon of Trent">Canon of Trent</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Vulgate" title="Vulgate">Vulgate</a> Bible <a href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic_Dogma" class="mw-redirect" title="Roman Catholic Dogma">dogma</a> in the Catholic Church. Later, <a href="/wiki/Pope_Pius_XI" title="Pope Pius XI">Pope Pius XI</a> on 2 June 1927 decreed the <a href="/wiki/Comma_Johanneum" class="mw-redirect" title="Comma Johanneum">Comma Johanneum</a> was open to dispute and <a href="/wiki/Pope_Pius_XII" title="Pope Pius XII">Pope Pius XII</a> on 3 September 1943 issued the encyclical <i><a href="/wiki/Divino_afflante_Spiritu" title="Divino afflante Spiritu">Divino afflante Spiritu</a></i>, which allowed translations based on other versions than just the Latin <a href="/wiki/Vulgate" title="Vulgate">Vulgate</a>, notably in English the <a href="/wiki/New_American_Bible" title="New American Bible">New American Bible</a>.
</p><p>Thus, some claim that, from the <a href="/wiki/Christianity_in_the_4th_century#Defining_scripture" title="Christianity in the 4th century">4th century</a>, there existed unanimity in the <a href="/wiki/Western_Church" class="mw-redirect" title="Western Church">West</a> concerning the New Testament canon (as it is today),<sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup> and that, by the <a href="/wiki/Christianity_in_the_5th_century" title="Christianity in the 5th century">5th century</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Church" class="mw-redirect" title="Eastern Church">Eastern Church</a>, with a few exceptions, had come to accept the <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Revelation" title="Book of Revelation">Book of Revelation</a> and thus had come into harmony on the matter of the canon.<sup id="cite_ref-P._R._Ackroyd_and_C._F._Evans,_eds._1970_305_139-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-P._R._Ackroyd_and_C._F._Evans,_eds._1970_305-139">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup> Nonetheless, full dogmatic articulations of the canon were not made until the <a href="/wiki/Canon_of_Trent" title="Canon of Trent">Canon of Trent</a> of 1546 for <a href="/wiki/Roman_Catholicism" class="mw-redirect" title="Roman Catholicism">Roman Catholicism</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Thirty-Nine_Articles" class="mw-redirect" title="Thirty-Nine Articles">Thirty-Nine Articles</a> of 1563 for the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_England" title="Church of England">Church of England</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Westminster_Confession_of_Faith" title="Westminster Confession of Faith">Westminster Confession of Faith</a> of 1647 for <a href="/wiki/Calvinism" title="Calvinism">Calvinism</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Synod_of_Jerusalem" class="mw-redirect" title="Synod of Jerusalem">Synod of Jerusalem</a> of 1672 for the <a href="/wiki/Greek_Orthodox" class="mw-redirect" title="Greek Orthodox">Greek Orthodox</a>.
</p><p>On the question of NT Canon formation generally, New Testament scholar Lee Martin McDonald has written that:<sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886047036"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Although a number of Christians have thought that <a href="/wiki/Church_council" class="mw-redirect" title="Church council">church councils</a> determined what books were to be included in the biblical canons, a more accurate reflection of the matter is that the councils recognized or acknowledged those books that had already obtained prominence from usage among the various early Christian communities.
</p></blockquote>
<p>Christian scholars assert that when these bishops and councils spoke on the matter, they were not defining something new, but instead "were ratifying what had already become the mind of the Church".<sup id="cite_ref-Metzger_1987_237–238_135-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Metzger_1987_237–238-135">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Bruce_136-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bruce-136">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Some synods of the 4th century published lists of canonical books (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Synod_of_Hippo" title="Synod of Hippo">Hippo</a> and <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Carthage" class="mw-redirect" title="Council of Carthage">Carthage</a>). The existing 27-book canon of the New Testament was reconfirmed (for <a href="/wiki/Roman_Catholicism" class="mw-redirect" title="Roman Catholicism">Roman Catholicism</a>) in the 16th century with the <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Trent" title="Council of Trent">Council of Trent</a> (also called the Tridentine Council) of 1546,<sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Thirty-Nine_Articles" class="mw-redirect" title="Thirty-Nine Articles">Thirty-Nine Articles</a> of 1563 for the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_England" title="Church of England">Church of England</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Westminster_Confession_of_Faith" title="Westminster Confession of Faith">Westminster Confession of Faith</a> of 1647 for <a href="/wiki/Calvinism" title="Calvinism">Calvinism</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Synod_of_Jerusalem" class="mw-redirect" title="Synod of Jerusalem">Synod of Jerusalem</a> of 1672 for <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Orthodoxy" class="mw-redirect" title="Eastern Orthodoxy">Eastern Orthodoxy</a>. Although these councils did include statements about the canon, when it came to the New Testament they were only reaffirming the existing canon, including the <a href="/wiki/Antilegomena" title="Antilegomena">Antilegomena</a>.
</p><p>According to the <i><a href="/wiki/Catholic_Encyclopedia" title="Catholic Encyclopedia">Catholic Encyclopedia</a></i> article on the Canon of the New Testament: "The idea of a complete and clear-cut canon of the New Testament existing from the beginning, that is from Apostolic times, has no foundation in history. The Canon of the New Testament, like that of the Old, is the result of a development, of a process at once stimulated by disputes with doubters, both within and without the Church, and retarded by certain obscurities and natural hesitations, and which did not reach its final term until the dogmatic definition of the <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Trent" title="Council of Trent">Tridentine Council</a>."<sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 331, <a href="/wiki/Constantine_I_and_Christianity" class="mw-redirect" title="Constantine I and Christianity">Constantine I</a> commissioned Eusebius to deliver <a href="/wiki/Fifty_Bibles_of_Constantine" title="Fifty Bibles of Constantine">fifty Bibles</a> for the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_Constantinople" class="mw-redirect" title="Church of Constantinople">Church of Constantinople</a>. <a href="/wiki/Athanasius" class="mw-redirect" title="Athanasius">Athanasius</a> (<i>Apol. Const. 4</i>) recorded Alexandrian scribes around 340 preparing Bibles for <a href="/wiki/Constans" title="Constans">Constans</a>. Little else is known, though there is plenty of speculation. For example, it is speculated that this may have provided motivation for canon lists, and that <a href="/wiki/Codex_Vaticanus_Graecus_1209" class="mw-redirect" title="Codex Vaticanus Graecus 1209">Codex Vaticanus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Codex_Sinaiticus" title="Codex Sinaiticus">Codex Sinaiticus</a> may be examples of these Bibles. Together with the <a href="/wiki/Peshitta" title="Peshitta">Peshitta</a> and <a href="/wiki/Codex_Alexandrinus" title="Codex Alexandrinus">Codex Alexandrinus</a>, these are the earliest extant Christian Bibles.<sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup> There is no evidence among the <a href="/wiki/First_Council_of_Nicaea#The_biblical_canon" title="First Council of Nicaea">canons of the First Council of Nicaea of any determination on the canon</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_manuscripts">Early manuscripts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Testament&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Early manuscripts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Papyrus_Bodmer_VIII.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Papyrus_Bodmer_VIII.jpg/220px-Papyrus_Bodmer_VIII.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="132" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Papyrus_Bodmer_VIII.jpg/330px-Papyrus_Bodmer_VIII.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Papyrus_Bodmer_VIII.jpg/440px-Papyrus_Bodmer_VIII.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2362" data-file-height="1417" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Papyrus_Bodmer_VIII.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Papyrus Bodmer VIII, at the Biblioteca Apostolica Vaticana, showing 1 and 2 Peter.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Codex_Regius_(019).JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Codex_Regius_%28019%29.JPG/220px-Codex_Regius_%28019%29.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="112" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Codex_Regius_%28019%29.JPG/330px-Codex_Regius_%28019%29.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Codex_Regius_%28019%29.JPG/440px-Codex_Regius_%28019%29.JPG 2x" data-file-width="630" data-file-height="320" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Codex_Regius_(019).JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Codex_Regius_(New_Testament)" title="Codex Regius (New Testament)">Codex Regius (L or <b>019</b>)</a>, an 8th-century Greek manuscript of the New Testament with strong affinities to <a href="/wiki/Codex_Vaticanus" title="Codex Vaticanus">Codex Vaticanus</a>.</div></div></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/New_Testament_manuscripts" class="mw-redirect" title="New Testament manuscripts">New Testament manuscripts</a></div>
<p>Like other literature from <a href="/wiki/Classical_antiquity" title="Classical antiquity">antiquity</a>, the text of the New Testament was (prior to the advent of the <a href="/wiki/Printing_press" title="Printing press">printing press</a>) preserved and transmitted in <a href="/wiki/Manuscripts" class="mw-redirect" title="Manuscripts">manuscripts</a>. Manuscripts containing at least a part of the New Testament number in the thousands. The earliest of these (like manuscripts containing other literature) are often very fragmentarily preserved. Some of these fragments have even been thought to date as early as the 2nd century (i.e., <a href="/wiki/Papyrus_90" title="Papyrus 90">Papyrus 90</a>, <a href="/wiki/Papyrus_98" title="Papyrus 98">Papyrus 98</a>, <a href="/wiki/Papyrus_104" title="Papyrus 104">Papyrus 104</a>, and famously <a href="/wiki/Rylands_Library_Papyrus_P52" title="Rylands Library Papyrus P52">Rylands Library Papyrus P52</a>, though the early date of the latter has recently been called into question).<sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>For each subsequent century, more and more manuscripts survive that contain a portion or all of the books that were held to be part of the New Testament at that time (for example, the New Testament of the 4th-century <a href="/wiki/Codex_Sinaiticus" title="Codex Sinaiticus">Codex Sinaiticus</a>, once a complete Bible, contains the <a href="/wiki/Epistle_of_Barnabas" title="Epistle of Barnabas">Epistle of Barnabas</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Shepherd_of_Hermas" class="mw-redirect" title="Shepherd of Hermas">Shepherd of Hermas</a>), though occasionally these manuscripts contain other works as well (e.g., <a href="/wiki/Papyrus_72" title="Papyrus 72">Papyrus 72</a> and the Crosby-Schøyen Codex). The date when a manuscript was written, however, does not necessarily reflect the date of the form of text it contains. That is, later manuscripts can, and occasionally do, contain older forms of text or older readings.
</p><p>Some of the more important manuscripts containing an early text of books of the New Testament are:
</p>
<ul><li>The <a href="/wiki/Chester_Beatty_Papyri" title="Chester Beatty Papyri">Chester Beatty Papyri</a> (Greek; the New Testament portions of which were copied in the 3rd century)</li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Bodmer_Papyri" title="Bodmer Papyri">Bodmer Papyri</a> (Greek and Coptic; the New Testament portions of which were copied in the 3rd and 4th centuries)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Codex_Bobiensis" title="Codex Bobiensis">Codex Bobiensis</a> (Latin; copied in the 4th century, but containing at least a 3rd-century form of text)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Uncial_0171" title="Uncial 0171">Uncial <b>0171</b></a> (Greek; copied in the late-third or early 4th century)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Syriac_Sinaiticus" title="Syriac Sinaiticus">Syriac Sinaiticus</a> (Syriac; copied in the 4th century)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Schoyen_Collection" class="mw-redirect" title="Schoyen Collection">Schøyen Manuscript</a> 2560 (Coptic; copied in the 4th century)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Codex_Vaticanus" title="Codex Vaticanus">Codex Vaticanus</a> (Greek; copied in the 4th century)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Codex_Sinaiticus" title="Codex Sinaiticus">Codex Sinaiticus</a> (Greek; copied in the 4th century)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Codex_Vercellensis" title="Codex Vercellensis">Codex Vercellensis</a> (Latin; copied in the 4th century)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Curetonian_Gospels" title="Curetonian Gospels">Curetonian Gospels</a> (Syriac; copied in the 5th century)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Garima_Gospels" title="Garima Gospels">Garima Gospels</a> ( <a href="/wiki/Ge%27ez_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Ge&#39;ez language">Ge'ez language</a>, produced in the 5th through 6th century)</li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Textual_variation">Textual variation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Testament&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Textual variation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Textual_variants_in_the_New_Testament" title="Textual variants in the New Testament">Textual variants in the New Testament</a> and <a href="/wiki/Textual_criticism_of_the_New_Testament" title="Textual criticism of the New Testament">Textual criticism of the New Testament</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Textual_criticism" title="Textual criticism">Textual criticism</a> deals with the identification and removal of <a href="/wiki/Transcription_(linguistics)" title="Transcription (linguistics)">transcription</a> errors in the <a href="/wiki/Writing" title="Writing">texts</a> of <a href="/wiki/Manuscripts" class="mw-redirect" title="Manuscripts">manuscripts</a>. Ancient <a href="/wiki/Scribes" class="mw-redirect" title="Scribes">scribes</a> made errors or alterations (such as including non-authentic <a href="/wiki/Interpolation_(manuscripts)" title="Interpolation (manuscripts)">additions</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup> The New Testament has been preserved in more than 5,800 <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a> manuscripts, 10,000 <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> manuscripts and 9,300 manuscripts in various other ancient languages including <a href="/wiki/Syriac_language" title="Syriac language">Syriac</a>, <a href="/wiki/Slavic_languages" title="Slavic languages">Slavic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ethiopic" class="mw-redirect" title="Ethiopic">Ethiopic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Armenian_language" title="Armenian language">Armenian</a>. Even if the <a href="/wiki/Greek_New_Testament" title="Greek New Testament">original Greek</a> versions were lost, the entire New Testament could still be assembled from the translations.<sup id="cite_ref-Strobel,_Lee_1998_146-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Strobel,_Lee_1998-146">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In addition, there are so many quotes from the New Testament in early church documents and commentaries that the entire New Testament could also be assembled from these alone.<sup id="cite_ref-Strobel,_Lee_1998_146-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Strobel,_Lee_1998-146">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup> Not all biblical manuscripts come from orthodox Christian writers. For example, the <a href="/wiki/Gnostic" class="mw-redirect" title="Gnostic">Gnostic</a> writings of <a href="/wiki/Valentinus_(Gnostic)" title="Valentinus (Gnostic)">Valentinus</a> come from the 2nd century AD, and these Christians were regarded as heretics by the mainstream church.<sup id="cite_ref-Bruce,_F.F._1981_P_14_147-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bruce,_F.F._1981_P_14-147">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup> The sheer number of witnesses presents unique difficulties, but it also gives scholars a better idea of how close modern Bibles are to the original versions.<sup id="cite_ref-Bruce,_F.F._1981_P_14_147-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bruce,_F.F._1981_P_14-147">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On noting the large number of surviving ancient manuscripts, <a href="/wiki/Bruce_Metzger" class="mw-redirect" title="Bruce Metzger">Bruce Metzger</a> sums up the view on the issue by saying "The more often you have copies that agree with each other, especially if they emerge from different geographical areas, the more you can cross-check them to figure out what the original document was like. The only way they'd agree would be where they went back genealogically in a family tree that represents the descent of the manuscripts.<sup id="cite_ref-Strobel,_Lee_1998_146-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Strobel,_Lee_1998-146">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Interpolations">Interpolations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Testament&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Interpolations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In attempting to determine the original text of the New Testament books, some modern textual critics have identified sections as additions of material, centuries after the gospel was written. These are called <a href="/wiki/Interpolation_(manuscripts)" title="Interpolation (manuscripts)">interpolations</a>. In modern translations of the Bible, the results of textual criticism have led to certain verses, words and phrases being left out or marked as not original. According to <a href="/wiki/Bart_D._Ehrman" title="Bart D. Ehrman">Bart D. Ehrman</a>, "These scribal additions are often found in late medieval manuscripts of the New Testament, but not in the manuscripts of the earlier centuries."<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_148-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-148">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Most modern Bibles have footnotes to indicate passages that have disputed source documents. Bible Commentaries also discuss these, sometimes in great detail. While many variations have been discovered between early copies of biblical texts, almost all have no importance, as they are variations in spelling, punctuation, or grammar. Also, many of these variants are so particular to the Greek language that they would not appear in translations into other languages. For example, order of words (i.e. "man bites dog" versus "dog bites man") often does not matter in Greek, so textual variants that flip the order of words often have no consequences.<sup id="cite_ref-Strobel,_Lee_1998_146-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Strobel,_Lee_1998-146">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Outside of these unimportant variants, there are a couple variants of some importance. The two most commonly cited examples are the <a href="/wiki/Mark_16" title="Mark 16">last verses</a> of the <i>Gospel of Mark</i><sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup> and the story of the <a href="/wiki/Pericope_Adulterae" class="mw-redirect" title="Pericope Adulterae">adulterous woman</a> in the <i>Gospel of John</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Oxford_154-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Oxford-154">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup> Many scholars and critics also believe that the <a href="/wiki/Comma_Johanneum" class="mw-redirect" title="Comma Johanneum">Comma Johanneum</a> reference supporting the <a href="/wiki/Trinity" title="Trinity">Trinity</a> doctrine in <a href="/wiki/1_John" class="mw-redirect" title="1 John">1 John</a> to have been a later addition.<sup id="cite_ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Metzger1994_156-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Metzger1994-156">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup> According to <a href="/wiki/Norman_Geisler" title="Norman Geisler">Norman Geisler</a> and William Nix, "The New Testament, then, has not only survived in more manuscripts than any other book from antiquity, but it has survived in a purer form than any other great book—a form that is 99.5% pure"<sup id="cite_ref-157" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-157">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:RossanoGospelsChristBeforePilate.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/RossanoGospelsChristBeforePilate.jpg/220px-RossanoGospelsChristBeforePilate.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="267" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/RossanoGospelsChristBeforePilate.jpg/330px-RossanoGospelsChristBeforePilate.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/RossanoGospelsChristBeforePilate.jpg/440px-RossanoGospelsChristBeforePilate.jpg 2x" data-file-width="524" data-file-height="637" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:RossanoGospelsChristBeforePilate.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Rossano_Gospels" title="Rossano Gospels">Rossano Gospels</a>, 6th century, a representative of <a href="/wiki/Byzantine" class="mw-redirect" title="Byzantine">Byzantine</a> text.</div></div></div>
<p>The often referred to Interpreter's Dictionary of the Bible, a book written to prove the validity of the New Testament, says: " A study of 150 Greek [manuscripts] of the Gospel of Luke has revealed more than 30,000 different readings... It is safe to say that there is not one sentence in the New Testament in which the [manuscript] is wholly uniform."<sup id="cite_ref-158" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-158">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup> Most of the variation took place within the first three Christian centuries.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Text-types">Text-types</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Testament&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Text-types">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>By the 4th century, textual "families" or types of text become discernible among <a href="/wiki/Biblical_manuscript" title="Biblical manuscript">New Testament manuscripts</a>. A "text-type" is the name given to a family of texts with similar readings due to common ancestors and mutual correction. Many early manuscripts, however, contain individual readings from several different earlier forms of text. Modern texual critics have identified the following text-types among textual witnesses to the New Testament: The <a href="/wiki/Alexandrian_text-type" title="Alexandrian text-type">Alexandrian text-type</a> is usually considered to generally preserve many early readings. It is represented, e.g., by <a href="/wiki/Codex_Vaticanus_Graecus_1209" class="mw-redirect" title="Codex Vaticanus Graecus 1209">Codex Vaticanus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Codex_Sinaiticus" title="Codex Sinaiticus">Codex Sinaiticus</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Bodmer_Papyri" title="Bodmer Papyri">Bodmer Papyri</a>.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Western_text-type" title="Western text-type">Western text-type</a> is generally longer and can be paraphrastic, but can also preserve early readings. The <a href="/wiki/Acts_of_the_Apostles#Manuscripts" title="Acts of the Apostles">Western version of the Acts of the Apostles</a> is, notably, 8.5% longer than the Alexandrian form of the text. Examples of the Western text are found in <a href="/wiki/Codex_Bezae" title="Codex Bezae">Codex Bezae</a>, <a href="/wiki/Codex_Claromontanus" title="Codex Claromontanus">Codex Claromontanus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Codex_Washingtonianus" title="Codex Washingtonianus">Codex Washingtonianus</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Vetus_Latina" title="Vetus Latina">Old Latin</a> (i.e., Latin translations made prior to the <a href="/wiki/Vulgate" title="Vulgate">Vulgate</a>), as well as in quotations by <a href="/wiki/Marcion" class="mw-redirect" title="Marcion">Marcion</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tatian" title="Tatian">Tatian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Irenaeus" title="Irenaeus">Irenaeus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tertullian" title="Tertullian">Tertullian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cyprian" title="Cyprian">Cyprian</a>.
</p><p>A text-type referred to as the "<a href="/wiki/Caesarean_text-type" title="Caesarean text-type">Caesarean text-type</a>" and thought to have included witnesses such as <a href="/wiki/Codex_Koridethi" title="Codex Koridethi">Codex Koridethi</a> and minuscule 565, can today be described neither as "Caesarean" nor as a text-type as was previously thought. However, the Gospel of Mark in <a href="/wiki/Papyrus_45" title="Papyrus 45">Papyrus 45</a>, <a href="/wiki/Codex_Washingtonianus" title="Codex Washingtonianus">Codex Washingtonianus</a> and in <a href="/wiki/Family_13" title="Family 13">Family 13</a> does indeed reflect a distinct type of text.
</p><p>Increasing standardization of distinct (and once local) text-types eventually gave rise to the <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_text-type" title="Byzantine text-type">Byzantine text-type</a>. Since most manuscripts of the New Testament do not derive from the first several centuries, that is, they were copied after the rise of the Byzantine text-type, this form of text is found the majority of extant manuscripts and is therefore often called the "Majority Text." As with all of the other (earlier) text-types, the Byzantine can also occasionally preserve early readings.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Biblical_criticism">Biblical criticism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Testament&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Biblical criticism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Biblical_criticism" title="Biblical criticism">Biblical criticism</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Biblical_criticism" title="Biblical criticism">Biblical criticism</a> is the scholarly "study and investigation of <a href="/wiki/Biblical_manuscript" title="Biblical manuscript">biblical writings</a> that seeks to make discerning judgments about these writings." Viewing biblical texts as having human rather than supernatural origins, it asks when and where a particular text originated; how, why, by whom, for whom, and in what circumstances it was produced; what influences were at work in its production; what sources were used in its composition; and what message it was intended to convey.
</p><p>It will vary slightly depending on whether the focus is on the <a href="/wiki/Old_Testament" title="Old Testament">Old Testament</a>, the letters of the New Testament, or the <a href="/wiki/Canonical_Gospels" class="mw-redirect" title="Canonical Gospels">Canonical Gospels</a>. It also plays an important role in the quest for the <a href="/wiki/Historical_Jesus" title="Historical Jesus">historical Jesus</a>. It also addresses the physical text, including the meaning of the words and the way in which they are used, its preservation, history, and integrity. Biblical criticism draws upon a wide range of scholarly disciplines including <a href="/wiki/Archaeology" title="Archaeology">archaeology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Anthropology" title="Anthropology">anthropology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Folklore" title="Folklore">folklore</a>, <a href="/wiki/Linguistics" title="Linguistics">linguistics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Christian_Oral_Tradition" class="mw-redirect" title="Christian Oral Tradition">Oral Tradition studies</a>, history, and <a href="/wiki/Religious_studies" title="Religious studies">religious studies</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Establishing_a_critical_text">Establishing a critical text</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Testament&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Establishing a critical text">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/New_Testament_manuscripts" class="mw-redirect" title="New Testament manuscripts">New Testament manuscripts</a></div>
<p>The <a href="#Textual_variation">textual variation</a> among manuscript copies of books in the New Testament prompted attempts to discern the earliest form of text already in antiquity (e.g., by the 3rd-century Christian author <a href="/wiki/Origen" title="Origen">Origen</a>). The efforts began in earnest again during the <a href="/wiki/Renaissance" title="Renaissance">Renaissance</a>, which saw a revival of the study of ancient Greek texts. During this period, modern <a href="/wiki/Textual_criticism" title="Textual criticism">textual criticism</a> was born. In this context, <a href="/wiki/Christian_humanism" title="Christian humanism">Christian humanists</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Lorenzo_Valla" title="Lorenzo Valla">Lorenzo Valla</a> and <a href="/wiki/Erasmus" title="Erasmus">Erasmus</a> promoted a return to the original Greek of the New Testament. This was the beginning of modern <a href="/wiki/New_Testament_textual_criticism" class="mw-redirect" title="New Testament textual criticism">New Testament textual criticism</a>, which over subsequent centuries would increasingly incorporate more and more manuscripts, in more languages (i.e., versions of the New Testament), as well as citations of the New Testament by ancient authors and the New Testament text in <a href="/wiki/Lectionary" title="Lectionary">lectionaries</a> in order to reconstruct the earliest recoverable form of the New Testament text and the history of changes to it.<sup id="cite_ref-159" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-159">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Relationship_to_earlier_and_contemporaneous_literature">Relationship to earlier and contemporaneous literature</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Testament&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Relationship to earlier and contemporaneous literature">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Non-canonical_books_referenced_in_the_Bible" title="Non-canonical books referenced in the Bible">Non-canonical books referenced in the Bible</a></div>
<p>Books that later formed the New Testament, like other Christian literature of the period, originated in a literary context that reveals relationships not only to other Christian writings, but also to <a href="/wiki/Classics" title="Classics">Graeco-Roman</a> and <a href="/wiki/Judaism#Antiquity" title="Judaism">Jewish</a> works. Of singular importance is the extensive use of and interaction with the <a href="/wiki/Tanakh" class="mw-redirect" title="Tanakh">Jewish Bible</a> and what would become the <a href="/wiki/Old_Testament" title="Old Testament">Christian Old Testament</a>. Both implicit and explicit citations, as well as countless allusions, appear throughout the books of the New Testament, from the Gospels and Acts, to the Epistles, to the Apocalypse.<sup id="cite_ref-160" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-160">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_versions">Early versions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Testament&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Early versions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The first translations (usually called "versions") of the New Testament were made beginning already at the end of 2nd century. The earliest versions of the New Testament are the translations into the <a href="/wiki/Syriac_language" title="Syriac language">Syriac</a>, <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Coptic_language" title="Coptic language">Coptic</a> languages.<sup id="cite_ref-161" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-161">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup> These three versions were made directly from the Greek, and are frequently cited in the apparatuses of modern critical editions.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Syriac">Syriac</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Testament&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Syriac">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Syriac_versions_of_the_Bible" title="Syriac versions of the Bible">Syriac versions of the Bible</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:RabulaGospelsFolio04vCanonTable.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/RabulaGospelsFolio04vCanonTable.jpg/220px-RabulaGospelsFolio04vCanonTable.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="268" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/RabulaGospelsFolio04vCanonTable.jpg/330px-RabulaGospelsFolio04vCanonTable.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/RabulaGospelsFolio04vCanonTable.jpg/440px-RabulaGospelsFolio04vCanonTable.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1445" data-file-height="1761" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:RabulaGospelsFolio04vCanonTable.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Rabbula_Gospels" title="Rabbula Gospels">Rabbula Gospels</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eusebian_Canons" title="Eusebian Canons">Eusebian Canons</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Syriac was spoken in <a href="/wiki/Syria" title="Syria">Syria</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">Mesopotamia</a>, and with dialect in <a href="/wiki/Syria_Palaestina" title="Syria Palaestina">Roman</a> and <a href="/wiki/Palaestina_Secunda" title="Palaestina Secunda">Byzantine Palestine</a> where it was known as <a href="/wiki/Jewish_Palestinian_Aramaic" title="Jewish Palestinian Aramaic">Jewish Palestinian Aramaic</a>. Several Syriac translations were made and have come to us. Most of the Old Syriac, however, as well as the Philoxonian version have been lost.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Tatian" title="Tatian">Tatian</a>, the Assyrian, created the <a href="/wiki/Diatessaron" title="Diatessaron">Diatessaron</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Gospel_harmony" title="Gospel harmony">gospel harmony</a> written in Syriac around 170 AD and the earliest form of the gospel not only in Syriac but probably also in Armenian.
</p><p>In the 19th century, manuscript evidence was discovered for an "Old Syriac" version of the four distinct (i.e., not harmonized) gospels. These "separated" (Syriac: <i>da-Mepharreshe</i>) gospels, though old, have been shown to be later than the Diatessaron. The Old Syriac gospels are fragmentarily preserved in two manuscripts: the 5th-century <a href="/wiki/Curetonian_Gospels" title="Curetonian Gospels">Curetonian Syriac</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Syriac_Sinaiticus" title="Syriac Sinaiticus">Sinaitic Syriac</a> from the 4th or 5th century.
</p><p>No Old Syriac manuscripts of other portions of the New Testament survive, though Old Syriac readings, e.g. from the <a href="/wiki/Pauline_Epistles" class="mw-redirect" title="Pauline Epistles">Pauline Epistles</a>, can be discerned in citations made by Eastern fathers and in later Syriac versions. The Old Syriac version is a representative of the <a href="/wiki/Western_text-type" title="Western text-type">Western text-type</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Peshitta" title="Peshitta">Peshitta</a> version was prepared in the beginning of the 5th century. It contains only 22 books (neither the <a href="/wiki/General_epistles" class="mw-redirect" title="General epistles">Minor Catholic Epistles</a> of 2 Peter, 2 and 3 John, and Jude, nor the <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Revelation" title="Book of Revelation">Book of Revelation</a> were part of this translation).
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Philoxenian" class="mw-redirect" title="Philoxenian">Philoxenian</a> probably was produced in 508 for <a href="/wiki/Philoxenus,_Bishop_of_Mabung" class="mw-redirect" title="Philoxenus, Bishop of Mabung">Philoxenus, Bishop of Mabung</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-162" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-162">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Latin">Latin</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Testament&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Latin">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Vetus_Latina" title="Vetus Latina">Vetus Latina</a> and <a href="/wiki/Vulgate" title="Vulgate">Vulgate</a></div>
<p>The Gospels were likely translated into Latin as early as the last quarter of the 2nd century in North Africa (<i>Afra</i>). Not much later, there were also European Latin translations (<i>Itala</i>). There are about 80 Old Latin mansucripts. The <a href="/wiki/Vetus_Latina" title="Vetus Latina">Vetus Latina</a> ("Old Latin") versions often contain readings with a Western type of text. (For the avoidance of confusion, these texts were written in <a href="/wiki/Late_Latin" title="Late Latin">Late Latin</a>, not the early version of the Latin language known as <a href="/wiki/Old_Latin" title="Old Latin">Old Latin</a>, pre 75 BC.)
</p><p>The bewildering diversity of the Old Latin versions prompted <a href="/wiki/Jerome" title="Jerome">Jerome</a> to prepare another translation into Latin—the <a href="/wiki/Vulgate" title="Vulgate">Vulgate</a>. In many respects it was merely a revision of the Old Latin. There are currently around 8,000 manuscripts of the Vulgate.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Coptic">Coptic</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Testament&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Coptic">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Coptic_versions_of_the_Bible" title="Coptic versions of the Bible">Coptic versions of the Bible</a></div>
<p>There are several dialects of the Coptic language: <a href="/wiki/Bohairic" class="mw-redirect" title="Bohairic">Bohairic</a> (northern dialect), <a href="/wiki/Fayyumic" class="mw-redirect" title="Fayyumic">Fayyumic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sahidic" class="mw-redirect" title="Sahidic">Sahidic</a> (southern dialect), <a href="/wiki/Akhmimic" class="mw-redirect" title="Akhmimic">Akhmimic</a>, and others. The first translation was made by at least the 3rd century into the Sahidic dialect (cop<sup>sa</sup>). This translation represents a mixed text, mostly <a href="/wiki/Alexandrian_text-type" title="Alexandrian text-type">Alexandrian</a>, though also with <a href="/wiki/Western_text-type" title="Western text-type">Western</a> readings.<sup id="cite_ref-163" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-163">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A Bohairic translation was made later, but existed already in the 4th century. Though the translation makes less use of Greek words than the Sahidic, it does employ some Greek grammar (e.g., in word-order and the use of particles such as the syntactic construction μεν—δε). For this reason, the Bohairic translation can be helpful in the reconstruction of the early Greek text of the New Testament.<sup id="cite_ref-164" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-164">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_ancient_translations">Other ancient translations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Testament&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: Other ancient translations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Matthew%27s_Gospel_-_British_Library_Add._MS_59874_Ethiopian_Bible.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Matthew%27s_Gospel_-_British_Library_Add._MS_59874_Ethiopian_Bible.jpg/220px-Matthew%27s_Gospel_-_British_Library_Add._MS_59874_Ethiopian_Bible.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="235" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Matthew%27s_Gospel_-_British_Library_Add._MS_59874_Ethiopian_Bible.jpg/330px-Matthew%27s_Gospel_-_British_Library_Add._MS_59874_Ethiopian_Bible.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Matthew%27s_Gospel_-_British_Library_Add._MS_59874_Ethiopian_Bible.jpg/440px-Matthew%27s_Gospel_-_British_Library_Add._MS_59874_Ethiopian_Bible.jpg 2x" data-file-width="844" data-file-height="900" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Matthew%27s_Gospel_-_British_Library_Add._MS_59874_Ethiopian_Bible.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>BL Add. MS 59874 with <a href="/wiki/Ethiopic" class="mw-redirect" title="Ethiopic">Ethiopic</a> <a href="/wiki/Gospel_of_Matthew" title="Gospel of Matthew">Gospel of Matthew</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>The continued spread of Christianity, and the foundation of national churches, led to the translation of the Bible—often beginning with books from the New Testament—into a variety of other languages at a relatively early date: <a href="/wiki/Bible_translations_into_Armenian" title="Bible translations into Armenian">Armenian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bible_translations_into_Georgian" title="Bible translations into Georgian">Georgian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ethiopic" class="mw-redirect" title="Ethiopic">Ethiopic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bible_translations_into_Persian" title="Bible translations into Persian">Persian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bible_translations_into_Sogdian" title="Bible translations into Sogdian">Sogdian</a>, and eventually <a href="/wiki/Gothic_Bible" title="Gothic Bible">Gothic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bible_translations_into_Church_Slavonic" title="Bible translations into Church Slavonic">Old Church Slavonic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bible_translations_into_Arabic" title="Bible translations into Arabic">Arabic</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Bible_translations_into_Nubian" title="Bible translations into Nubian">Nubian</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-165" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-165">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_translations">Modern translations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Testament&amp;action=edit&amp;section=41" title="Edit section: Modern translations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Bible_translations" title="Bible translations">Bible translations</a></div>
<p>Historically, throughout the <a href="/wiki/Christendom" title="Christendom">Christian world</a> and in the context of <a href="/wiki/Mission_(Christian)" class="mw-redirect" title="Mission (Christian)">Christian missionary activity</a>, the New Testament (or portions thereof) has been that part of the Christian Bible first translated into the <a href="/wiki/Vernacular" title="Vernacular">vernacular</a>. The production of such translations grew out of the insertion of <a href="/wiki/Vernacular" title="Vernacular">vernacular</a> <a href="/wiki/Gloss_(annotation)" title="Gloss (annotation)">glosses</a> in biblical texts, as well as out of the production of <a href="/wiki/Biblical_paraphrase" title="Biblical paraphrase">biblical paraphrases</a> and poetic renditions of stories from the life of Christ (e.g., the <a href="/wiki/Heliand" title="Heliand">Heliand</a>).
</p><p>The 16th century saw the rise of <a href="/wiki/Protestantism" title="Protestantism">Protestantism</a> and an explosion of translations of the New (and Old) Testament into the <a href="/wiki/Vernacular" title="Vernacular">vernacular</a>.
